From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia

Number, approximately 3.14

This article is about the mathematical constant. For the Greek letter, see [Pi (letter)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi_(letter) "Pi (letter)"). For other uses, see [Pi (disambiguation)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi_(disambiguation) "Pi (disambiguation)") and [PI (disambiguation)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/PI_(disambiguation) "PI (disambiguation)").

[![A unit circle is rolled across a surface; after a full revolution, the center of the circle has moved π units forwards.](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Pi-unrolled-720.gif/500px-Pi-unrolled-720.gif)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Pi-unrolled-720.gif)[![Single frame of a rolling unit circle](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Pi-unrolled-720-frame.png/500px-Pi-unrolled-720-frame.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Pi-unrolled-720-frame.png)

An illustration of the value of pi

Animated

Static

The number **π** ([/paɪ/](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Help:IPA/English "Help:IPA/English")[Play audio](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/5/5e/LL-Q1860_%28eng%29-Flame%2C_not_lame-Pi.wav/LL-Q1860_%28eng%29-Flame%2C_not_lame-Pi.wav.mp3 "Play audio")[ⓘ](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:LL-Q1860_(eng)-Flame,_not_lame-Pi.wav "File:LL-Q1860 (eng)-Flame, not lame-Pi.wav"); spelled out as **pi**) is a [mathematical constant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematical_constant "Mathematical constant"), approximately equal to 3.14159, that is the [ratio](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ratio "Ratio") of a [circle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Circle "Circle")'s [circumference](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Circumference "Circumference") to its [diameter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Diameter "Diameter"). It appears in many [formulae](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Formulae "Formulae") across [mathematics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics "Mathematics") and [physics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Physics "Physics"), and some of these formulae are commonly used for defining π, to avoid relying on the definition of the [length of a curve](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arc_length "Arc length").

The number π is an [irrational number](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Irrational_number "Irrational number"), meaning that it cannot be expressed exactly as a ratio of two [integers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Integer "Integer"), although fractions such as ⁠22/7⁠ are commonly [used to approximate it](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Approximations_of_%CF%80 "Approximations of π"). Consequently, its [decimal representation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Decimal_representation "Decimal representation") never ends, nor does it [enter a permanently repeating pattern](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Repeating_decimal "Repeating decimal"). It is a [transcendental number](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transcendental_number "Transcendental number"), meaning that it cannot be a solution of an [algebraic equation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Algebraic_equation "Algebraic equation") involving only finite sums, [products](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Product_(mathematics) "Product (mathematics)"), [powers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Exponentiation "Exponentiation"), and integers. The transcendence of π implies that it is impossible to solve the ancient problem of [squaring the circle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Squaring_the_circle "Squaring the circle") with a [compass and straightedge](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Compass-and-straightedge_construction "Compass-and-straightedge construction"). The decimal digits of π appear to be [evenly distributed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Normal_number "Normal number"), but no proof of this [conjecture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conjecture "Conjecture") has been found.

Mathematicians have attempted to extend their understanding of π, sometimes by computing its value to a high degree of accuracy. Ancient civilizations, including the [Egyptians](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Egyptian_mathematics "Egyptian mathematics") and [Babylonians](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Babylonian_mathematics "Babylonian mathematics"), required fairly accurate approximations of π for practical computations. Around 250BC, the [Greek mathematician](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greek_mathematics "Greek mathematics") [Archimedes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archimedes "Archimedes") created an [algorithm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Algorithm "Algorithm") to approximate π with arbitrary accuracy. In the 5th century AD, [Chinese mathematicians](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chinese_mathematics "Chinese mathematics") approximated π to seven digits, while [Indian mathematicians](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indian_mathematics "Indian mathematics") made a five-digit approximation, both using [geometrical](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geometrical "Geometrical") techniques. The first computational formula for π, based on [infinite series](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Series_(mathematics) "Series (mathematics)"), was discovered a millennium later.

The earliest known use of the Greek letter [π](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi_(letter) "Pi (letter)") to represent the ratio of a circle's circumference to its diameter was by the Welsh mathematician [William Jones](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/William_Jones_(mathematician) "William Jones (mathematician)") in 1706. The invention of [calculus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Calculus "Calculus") soon led to the calculation of hundreds of digits of π, enough for all practical scientific computations. Nevertheless, in the 20th and 21st centuries, mathematicians and [computer scientists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Computer_science "Computer science") have pursued new approaches that, when combined with increasing computational power, extended the decimal representation of π to hundreds of trillions of digits. These computations are motivated by the development of efficient algorithms to calculate numeric series, as well as the human quest to break records. The extensive computations involved have also been used to test the correctness of new computer processors.

Because it relates to a circle, π is found in formulae in [trigonometry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trigonometry "Trigonometry") and geometry, especially those concerning circles, [ellipses](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ellipse "Ellipse") and [spheres](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sphere "Sphere"). It is found as well in formulae from [cosmology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cosmology "Cosmology"), [fractals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fractal "Fractal"), [thermodynamics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thermodynamics "Thermodynamics"), [mechanics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mechanics "Mechanics"), and [electromagnetism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electromagnetism "Electromagnetism"). It also appears in areas such as [number theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Number_theory "Number theory"), [statistics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Statistics "Statistics"), and in modern [mathematical analysis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematical_analysis "Mathematical analysis"): π is ubiquitous.

## Fundamentals

### Name

The symbol used by mathematicians to represent the ratio of a circle's circumference to its diameter is the lowercase [Greek letter π](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi_(letter) "Pi (letter)"), sometimes spelled out as _pi_.[\[1\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-firstPi-1) In English, π is [pronounced as "pie"](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/English_pronunciation_of_Greek_letters "English pronunciation of Greek letters") ([/paɪ/](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Help:IPA/English "Help:IPA/English") [_PY_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key "Help:Pronunciation respelling key")).[\[2\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-2) In mathematical use, the lowercase letter π is distinguished from its capitalized and enlarged counterpart Π, which denotes a [product of a sequence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Multiplication#Product_of_a_sequence "Multiplication"), analogously to how Σ denotes [summation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Summation "Summation").

The choice of the symbol π is discussed in the section [§Adoption of the symbol π](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#Adoption_of_the_symbol_%CF%80).

### Definition

[![A diagram of a circle, with the width labelled as diameter, and the perimeter labelled as circumference](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Pi_eq_C_over_d.svg/250px-Pi_eq_C_over_d.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Pi_eq_C_over_d.svg) The circumference of a circle is slightly more than three times as long as its diameter. The exact ratio is called π.

π is commonly defined as the [ratio](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ratio "Ratio") of a [circle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Circle "Circle")'s [circumference](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Circumference "Circumference") C to its [diameter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Diameter "Diameter") d:
π=Cd.{\\displaystyle \\pi ={\\frac {C}{d}}.}![{\displaystyle \pi ={\frac {C}{d}}.}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f98a23e73a342246e95838018afd6f157a859564)
In [Euclidean geometry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Euclidean_geometry "Euclidean geometry"), the ratio Cd{\\textstyle {\\frac {C}{d}}}![{\textstyle {\frac {C}{d}}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c8ebfc4cd5de67be49da2841f490d1d55053a1c4) is constant, regardless of the circle's size. For example, if a circle has twice the diameter of another circle, it will also have twice the circumference, preserving the ratio Cd{\\textstyle {\\frac {C}{d}}}![{\textstyle {\frac {C}{d}}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c8ebfc4cd5de67be49da2841f490d1d55053a1c4).[\[3\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel20068-3)

Here, the circumference of a circle is the [arc length](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arc_length "Arc length") around the [perimeter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Perimeter "Perimeter") of the circle, a quantity which can be formally defined using [limits](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Limit_(mathematics) "Limit (mathematics)") – a concept in [calculus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Calculus "Calculus").[\[4\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-4) For example, one may directly compute the arc length of the top half of the unit circle, given in [Cartesian coordinates](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cartesian_coordinates "Cartesian coordinates") by the equation x2+y2=1{\\textstyle x^{2}+y^{2}=1}![{\textstyle x^{2}+y^{2}=1}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/1207955c0a974b8d1849a63adbfb457d9fd4679c), as the [integral](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Integral "Integral"):[\[5\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTERemmert2012129-5)π=∫−11dx1−x2.{\\displaystyle \\pi =\\int \_{-1}^{1}{\\frac {dx}{\\sqrt {1-x^{2}}}}.}![{\displaystyle \pi =\int _{-1}^{1}{\frac {dx}{\sqrt {1-x^{2}}}}.}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/50725fce8b20701f737a6ecc57aff4b8969107c4)

An integral such as this was proposed as a definition of π by [Karl Weierstrass](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Weierstrass "Karl Weierstrass"), who defined it directly as an integral in 1841.[\[a\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-7)

Integration is no longer commonly used in a first analytical definition because, as [Remmert 2012](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#CITEREFRemmert2012) explains, [differential calculus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Differential_calculus "Differential calculus") typically precedes integral calculus in the university curriculum, so it is desirable to have a definition of π that does not rely on the latter. One such definition, due to Richard Baltzer[\[7\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-8) and popularized by [Edmund Landau](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edmund_Landau "Edmund Landau"),[\[8\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-9) is the following: π is twice the smallest positive number at which the [cosine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cosine "Cosine") function equals 0.[\[3\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel20068-3)[\[5\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTERemmert2012129-5)[\[9\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-Rudin_1976-10)π is also the smallest positive number at which the [sine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sine "Sine") function equals zero, and the difference between consecutive zeroes of the sine function. The cosine and sine can be defined independently of geometry as a [power series](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Power_series "Power series"),[\[10\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-11) or as the solution of a [differential equation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Differential_equation "Differential equation").[\[9\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-Rudin_1976-10)

In a similar spirit, π can be defined using properties of the [complex exponential](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Complex_exponential "Complex exponential"), exp _z_, of a [complex](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Complex_number "Complex number") variable z. Like the cosine, the complex exponential can be defined in one of several ways. The set of complex numbers at which exp _z_ is equal to one is then an (imaginary) arithmetic progression of the form:
{…,−2πi,0,2πi,4πi,…}={2πki∣k∈Z}{\\displaystyle \\{\\dots ,-2\\pi i,0,2\\pi i,4\\pi i,\\dots \\}=\\{2\\pi ki\\mid k\\in \\mathbb {Z} \\}}![{\displaystyle \{\dots ,-2\pi i,0,2\pi i,4\pi i,\dots \}=\{2\pi ki\mid k\in \mathbb {Z} \}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9411506ae82c49d1b868a47ce5dea04adf234cba)
and there is a unique positive real number π with this property.[\[5\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTERemmert2012129-5)[\[11\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-12)

### Irrationality and normality

π is an [irrational number](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Irrational_number "Irrational number"), meaning that it cannot be written as the [ratio of two integers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rational_number "Rational number"). Fractions such as ⁠22/7⁠ and ⁠355/113⁠ are commonly used to approximate π, but no [common fraction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Common_fraction "Common fraction") (ratio of whole numbers) can be its exact value.[\[12\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel20065-13) Because π is irrational, it has an [infinite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Infinity "Infinity") number of digits in its [decimal representation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Decimal_representation "Decimal representation"), and does not settle into an infinitely [repeating pattern](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Repeating_decimal "Repeating decimal") of digits. There are several [proofs that π is irrational](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proof_that_%CF%80_is_irrational "Proof that π is irrational"); they are generally [proofs by contradiction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proofs_by_contradiction "Proofs by contradiction") and require calculus. The degree to which π can be approximated by [rational numbers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rational_number "Rational number") (called the [irrationality measure](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Irrationality_measure "Irrationality measure")) is not precisely known; estimates have established that the irrationality measure is larger or at least equal to the measure of e but smaller than the measure of [Liouville numbers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Liouville_number "Liouville number").[\[13\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-14)

Unsolved problem in mathematics

Prove or disprove that π{\\displaystyle \\pi }![{\displaystyle \pi }](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9be4ba0bb8df3af72e90a0535fabcc17431e540a) is [normal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Normal_number "Normal number")

[More unsolved problems in mathematics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_unsolved_problems_in_mathematics "List of unsolved problems in mathematics")

The digits of π have no apparent pattern and have passed tests for [statistical randomness](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Statistical_randomness "Statistical randomness"), including tests for [normality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Normal_number "Normal number"); a number of infinite length is called normal when all possible sequences of digits (of any given length) appear equally often. The conjecture that π is normal has not been proven or disproven.[\[14\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel200622%E2%80%9323-15)

Since the advent of computers, a large number of digits of π have been available on which to perform statistical analysis. [Yasumasa Kanada](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yasumasa_Kanada "Yasumasa Kanada") has performed detailed statistical analyses on the decimal digits of π, and found them consistent with normality; for example, the frequencies of the ten digits 0 to 9 were subjected to [statistical significance tests](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Statistical_significance_test "Statistical significance test"), and no evidence of a pattern was found.[\[15\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel200622,_28%E2%80%9330-16) Any random sequence of digits contains arbitrarily long subsequences that appear non-random, by the [infinite monkey theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Infinite_monkey_theorem "Infinite monkey theorem"). Thus, because the sequence of π's digits passes statistical tests for randomness, it contains some sequences of digits that may appear non-random, such as a [sequence of six consecutive 9s](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Six_nines_in_pi "Six nines in pi") that begins at the 762nd decimal place of the decimal representation of π.[\[16\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel20063-17)

### Transcendence

See also: [Lindemann–Weierstrass theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lindemann%E2%80%93Weierstrass_theorem "Lindemann–Weierstrass theorem")

[![A diagram of a square and circle, both with identical area; the length of the side of the square is the square root of pi](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Squaring_the_circle.svg/250px-Squaring_the_circle.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Squaring_the_circle.svg) Because π is a [transcendental number](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transcendental_number "Transcendental number"), [squaring the circle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Squaring_the_circle "Squaring the circle") is not possible in a finite number of steps using the classical tools of [compass and straightedge](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Compass-and-straightedge_construction "Compass-and-straightedge construction").

In addition to being irrational, π is also a [transcendental number](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transcendental_number "Transcendental number"), which means that it is not the [solution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solution_(equation) "Solution (equation)") of any non-constant [polynomial equation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polynomial_equation "Polynomial equation") with [rational](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rational_number "Rational number") coefficients, such as x5120−x36+x=0{\\textstyle {\\frac {x^{5}}{120}}-{\\frac {x^{3}}{6}}+x=0}![{\textstyle {\frac {x^{5}}{120}}-{\frac {x^{3}}{6}}+x=0}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/fcb75c4d7ee0e26b7281831ffb74ea6d2496b1b6).[\[17\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel20066%E2%80%937-18)[\[b\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-19) This follows from the so-called [Lindemann–Weierstrass theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lindemann%E2%80%93Weierstrass_theorem#Transcendence_of_e_and_%CF%80 "Lindemann–Weierstrass theorem"), which also establishes the transcendence of [the constant _e_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/E_(mathematical_constant) "E (mathematical constant)").[\[17\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel20066%E2%80%937-18)

The transcendence of π has two important consequences: First, π cannot be expressed using any finite combination of rational numbers and [square roots](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Square_root "Square root") or [_n_ th roots](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nth_root "Nth root") (such as 313{\\displaystyle {\\sqrt\[{3}\]{31}}}![{\displaystyle {\sqrt[{3}]{31}}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/1fb1cd8b2742403364f3adca4fc8b8ca4a21e7f5) or 10{\\displaystyle {\\sqrt {10}}}![{\displaystyle {\sqrt {10}}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/dd7409b0ddbc1f90280265e7bc95dd20626ebf1b)). Second, since no transcendental number can be [constructed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Constructible_number "Constructible number") with [compass and straightedge](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Compass-and-straightedge_construction "Compass-and-straightedge construction"), it is not possible to " [square the circle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Squaring_the_circle "Squaring the circle")". In other words, it is impossible to construct, using compass and straightedge alone, a square whose area is exactly equal to the area of a given circle.[\[18\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPosamentierLehmann200425-20) Squaring a circle was one of the important geometry problems of the [classical antiquity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Classical_antiquity "Classical antiquity").[\[19\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEymardLafon2004129-21) Amateur mathematicians in modern times have sometimes attempted to square the circle and claim success – despite the fact that it is mathematically impossible.[\[20\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-22)

An [unsolved problem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_unsolved_problems_in_mathematics "List of unsolved problems in mathematics") thus far is the question of whether or not the numbers π and e are [algebraically independent](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Algebraic_independence "Algebraic independence") ("relatively transcendental"). This would be resolved by [Schanuel's conjecture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Schanuel%27s_conjecture "Schanuel's conjecture")[\[21\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-23) – a currently unproven generalization of the Lindemann–Weierstrass theorem.[\[22\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-24)

### Continued fractions

As an irrational number, π cannot be represented as a [common fraction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Common_fraction "Common fraction"). However, like every number, π can be represented by an infinite series of nested fractions, called a [simple continued fraction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Simple_continued_fraction "Simple continued fraction"):

π=3+17+115+11+1292+11+11+11+⋱{\\displaystyle \\pi =3+\\textstyle {\\cfrac {1}{7+\\textstyle {\\cfrac {1}{15+\\textstyle {\\cfrac {1}{1+\\textstyle {\\cfrac {1}{292+\\textstyle {\\cfrac {1}{1+\\textstyle {\\cfrac {1}{1+\\textstyle {\\cfrac {1}{1+\\ddots }}}}}}}}}}}}}}}![{\displaystyle \pi =3+\textstyle {\cfrac {1}{7+\textstyle {\cfrac {1}{15+\textstyle {\cfrac {1}{1+\textstyle {\cfrac {1}{292+\textstyle {\cfrac {1}{1+\textstyle {\cfrac {1}{1+\textstyle {\cfrac {1}{1+\ddots }}}}}}}}}}}}}}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e294255007892e9424e6d3f49183cad6b133bbcf)

Truncating the continued fraction at any point yields a rational approximation for π; the first four of these are 3, ⁠22/7⁠, ⁠333/106⁠, and ⁠355/113⁠. These numbers are among the best-known and most widely used historical approximations of the constant. Each approximation generated in this way is a best rational approximation; that is, each is closer to π than any other fraction with the same or a smaller [denominator](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fraction "Fraction").[\[23\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEymardLafon200478-25) Because π is transcendental, it is by definition not [algebraic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Algebraic_number "Algebraic number") and so cannot be a [quadratic irrational](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quadratic_irrational "Quadratic irrational"). Therefore, π cannot have a [periodic continued fraction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Periodic_continued_fraction "Periodic continued fraction"). Although the simple continued fraction for π (with numerators all 1, shown above) also does not exhibit any other obvious pattern,[\[24\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel200633-26)[\[25\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-mollin-27) several non-simple [continued fractions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Continued_fraction "Continued fraction") do, such as:[\[26\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-28)[\[c\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-29)

π=3+126+326+526+726+⋱=41+122+322+522+⋱=41+123+225+327+⋱{\\displaystyle {\\begin{aligned}\\pi &=3+{\\cfrac {1^{2}}{6+{\\cfrac {3^{2}}{6+{\\cfrac {5^{2}}{6+{\\cfrac {7^{2}}{6+\\ddots }}}}}}}}={\\cfrac {4}{1+{\\cfrac {1^{2}}{2+{\\cfrac {3^{2}}{2+{\\cfrac {5^{2}}{2+\\ddots }}}}}}}}={\\cfrac {4}{1+{\\cfrac {1^{2}}{3+{\\cfrac {2^{2}}{5+{\\cfrac {3^{2}}{7+\\ddots }}}}}}}}\\end{aligned}}}![{\displaystyle {\begin{aligned}\pi &=3+{\cfrac {1^{2}}{6+{\cfrac {3^{2}}{6+{\cfrac {5^{2}}{6+{\cfrac {7^{2}}{6+\ddots }}}}}}}}={\cfrac {4}{1+{\cfrac {1^{2}}{2+{\cfrac {3^{2}}{2+{\cfrac {5^{2}}{2+\ddots }}}}}}}}={\cfrac {4}{1+{\cfrac {1^{2}}{3+{\cfrac {2^{2}}{5+{\cfrac {3^{2}}{7+\ddots }}}}}}}}\end{aligned}}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/02c2f88e03c466938a66d99b268ff5a5ea756dc1)

### Approximate value and digits

Some [approximations of pi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Approximations_of_%CF%80 "Approximations of π") include:

- **Integers**: 3
- **Fractions**: Approximate fractions include (in order of increasing accuracy) ⁠22/7⁠, ⁠333/106⁠, ⁠355/113⁠, ⁠52163/16604⁠, ⁠103993/33102⁠, ⁠104348/33215⁠, and ⁠245850922/78256779⁠.[\[23\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEymardLafon200478-25) (List is selected terms from [OEIS](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/On-Line_Encyclopedia_of_Integer_Sequences "On-Line Encyclopedia of Integer Sequences"): [A063674](https://oeis.org/A063674 "oeis:A063674") and [OEIS](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/On-Line_Encyclopedia_of_Integer_Sequences "On-Line Encyclopedia of Integer Sequences"): [A063673](https://oeis.org/A063673 "oeis:A063673").)
- **Digits**: The first 50 decimal digits are 3.14159265358979323846264338327950288419716939937510...[\[27\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006240-30) (see [OEIS](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/On-Line_Encyclopedia_of_Integer_Sequences "On-Line Encyclopedia of Integer Sequences"): [A000796](https://oeis.org/A000796 "oeis:A000796"))

**Digits in other number systems**

- The first 48 [binary](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Binary_number#Representing_real_numbers "Binary number") ( [base](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radix "Radix") 2) digits (called [bits](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bit "Bit")) are 11.001001000011111101101010100010001000010110100011... (see [OEIS](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/On-Line_Encyclopedia_of_Integer_Sequences "On-Line Encyclopedia of Integer Sequences"): [A004601](https://oeis.org/A004601 "oeis:A004601"))
- The first 36 digits in [ternary](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ternary_numeral_system "Ternary numeral system") (base 3) are 10.010211012222010211002111110221222220... (see [OEIS](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/On-Line_Encyclopedia_of_Integer_Sequences "On-Line Encyclopedia of Integer Sequences"): [A004602](https://oeis.org/A004602 "oeis:A004602"))
- The first 20 digits in [hexadecimal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hexadecimal "Hexadecimal") (base 16) are 3.243F6A8885A308D31319...[\[28\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006242-31) (see [OEIS](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/On-Line_Encyclopedia_of_Integer_Sequences "On-Line Encyclopedia of Integer Sequences"): [A062964](https://oeis.org/A062964 "oeis:A062964"))
- The first five [sexagesimal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexagesimal "Sexagesimal") (base 60) digits are 3;8,29,44,0,47[\[29\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-32) (see [OEIS](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/On-Line_Encyclopedia_of_Integer_Sequences "On-Line Encyclopedia of Integer Sequences"): [A060707](https://oeis.org/A060707 "oeis:A060707"))

### Complex numbers and Euler's identity

Main article: [Euler's identity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Euler%27s_identity "Euler's identity")

[![A diagram of a unit circle centred at the origin in the complex plane, including a ray from the centre of the circle to its edge, with the triangle legs labelled with sine and cosine functions.](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Euler%27s_formula.svg/250px-Euler%27s_formula.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Euler%27s_formula.svg) The association between imaginary powers of the number e and [points](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Point_(geometry) "Point (geometry)") on the [unit circle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Unit_circle "Unit circle") centred at the [origin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Origin_(mathematics) "Origin (mathematics)") in the [complex plane](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Complex_plane "Complex plane") given by [Euler's formula](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Euler%27s_formula "Euler's formula")

Any [complex number](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Complex_number "Complex number"), say z, can be expressed using a pair of [real numbers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Real_number "Real number"). In the [polar coordinate system](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polar_coordinate_system#Complex_numbers "Polar coordinate system"), one number ( [radius](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radius "Radius") or r) is used to represent z's distance from the [origin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Origin_(mathematics) "Origin (mathematics)") of the [complex plane](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Complex_plane "Complex plane"), and the other (angle or φ) the counter-clockwise [rotation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rotation "Rotation") from the positive real line:[\[30\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAbramson2014[httpsopenstaxorgbooksprecalculuspages8-5-polar-form-of-complex-numbers_Section_8.5:_Polar_form_of_complex_numbers]-33)

z=r⋅(cos⁡φ+isin⁡φ),{\\displaystyle z=r\\cdot (\\cos \\varphi +i\\sin \\varphi ),}![{\displaystyle z=r\cdot (\cos \varphi +i\sin \varphi ),}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/8e93279bb31b398ca08d16fb9d764fd1214174ab)

where i is the [imaginary unit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Imaginary_unit "Imaginary unit") satisfying i2=−1{\\displaystyle i^{2}=-1}![{\displaystyle i^{2}=-1}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/88e98a401d352e5037d5043028e2d7f449e83fa6). The frequent appearance of π in [complex analysis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Complex_analysis "Complex analysis") can be related to the behaviour of the [exponential function](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Exponential_function "Exponential function") of a complex variable, described by [Euler's formula](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Euler%27s_formula "Euler's formula"):[\[31\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBronshte%C4%ADnSemendiaev1971592-34)

eiφ=cos⁡φ+isin⁡φ,{\\displaystyle e^{i\\varphi }=\\cos \\varphi +i\\sin \\varphi ,}![{\displaystyle e^{i\varphi }=\cos \varphi +i\sin \varphi ,}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/3b85e2656dda51f900210b347fb30b65d3a26f11)

where [the constant e](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/E_(mathematical_constant) "E (mathematical constant)") is the base of the [natural logarithm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Natural_logarithm "Natural logarithm"). This formula establishes a correspondence between imaginary powers of e and points on the [unit circle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Unit_circle "Unit circle") centred at the origin of the complex plane. Setting φ=π{\\displaystyle \\varphi =\\pi }![{\displaystyle \varphi =\pi }](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/3f181a336ae10f53a432232ff83696fbdeb4e622) in Euler's formula results in [Euler's identity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Euler%27s_identity "Euler's identity"), celebrated in mathematics due to it containing five important mathematical constants:[\[31\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBronshte%C4%ADnSemendiaev1971592-34)[\[32\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-35)

eiπ+1=0.{\\displaystyle e^{i\\pi }+1=0.}![{\displaystyle e^{i\pi }+1=0.}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e76caf050d8bc37cd2350c40517face26de5ecb7)

There are n different complex numbers z satisfying ⁠zn=1{\\displaystyle z^{n}=1}![{\displaystyle z^{n}=1}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/34fb31edf665701c275c44a5be8b82a95509888d)⁠, and these are called the "nth [roots of unity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roots_of_unity "Roots of unity")"[\[33\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAndrewsAskeyRoy199914-36) and are given by the formula:

e2πik/n(k=0,1,2,…,n−1).{\\displaystyle e^{2\\pi ik/n}\\qquad (k=0,1,2,\\dots ,n-1).}![{\displaystyle e^{2\pi ik/n}\qquad (k=0,1,2,\dots ,n-1).}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c74d8393eb2cf4d7a3e5ef0dd9ab50a9fe58389f)

## History

Main article: [Approximations of pi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Approximations_of_pi "Approximations of pi")

See also: [Chronology of computation of pi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chronology_of_computation_of_pi "Chronology of computation of pi")

Surviving approximations of π prior to the 2nd centuryCE are accurate to one or two decimal places at best. The earliest written approximations are found in [Babylon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Babylon "Babylon") and [Egypt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ancient_Egypt "Ancient Egypt"), both within one percent of the true value. In Babylon, a [clay tablet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clay_tablet "Clay tablet") dated 1900–1600BCE has a geometrical statement that, by implication, treats π as ⁠25/8⁠ = 3.125.[\[34\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006167-37) In Egypt, the [Rhind Papyrus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rhind_Papyrus "Rhind Papyrus"), dated around [1650BCE](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Second_Intermediate_Period_of_Egypt "Second Intermediate Period of Egypt") but copied from a document dated to [1850BCE](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Middle_Kingdom_of_Egypt "Middle Kingdom of Egypt"), has a formula for the area of a circle that treats π as (169)2≈3.16{\\textstyle {\\bigl (}{\\frac {16}{9}}{\\bigr )}^{2}\\approx 3.16}![{\textstyle {\bigl (}{\frac {16}{9}}{\bigr )}^{2}\approx 3.16}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/1e578ff1826354c928fbf5aeef8660fcaa0f388d).[\[25\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-mollin-27)[\[34\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006167-37) Although some [pyramidologists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pyramidology "Pyramidology") have theorized that the [Great Pyramid of Giza](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_Pyramid_of_Giza "Great Pyramid of Giza") was built with proportions related to π, this theory is not widely accepted by scholars.[\[35\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-38)
In the [Shulba Sutras](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shulba_Sutras "Shulba Sutras") of [Indian mathematics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indian_mathematics "Indian mathematics"), dating to an oral tradition from the 1st or 2nd millenniumBCE, approximations are given which have been variously interpreted as approximately 3.08831, 3.08833, 3.004, 3, or 3.125.[\[36\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-39)

### Polygon approximation era

[![A painting of a man studying](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Domenico-Fetti_Archimedes_1620.jpg/250px-Domenico-Fetti_Archimedes_1620.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Domenico-Fetti_Archimedes_1620.jpg) [Archimedes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archimedes "Archimedes") developed the polygonal approach to approximating π.[![Diagram of a hexagon and pentagon circumscribed outside a circle](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Archimedes_pi.svg/250px-Archimedes_pi.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Archimedes_pi.svg) π can be estimated by computing the perimeters of circumscribed and inscribed polygons.

The first recorded algorithm for rigorously calculating the value of π was a geometrical approach using [polygons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polygon "Polygon"), devised around 250BC by the Greek mathematician [Archimedes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archimedes "Archimedes"), implementing the [method of exhaustion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Method_of_exhaustion "Method of exhaustion").[\[37\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006170-40) This polygonal algorithm dominated for over 1,000 years, and as a result π is sometimes referred to as Archimedes's constant.[\[38\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006175,_205-41) Archimedes computed upper and lower bounds of π by drawing a regular [hexagon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hexagon "Hexagon") inside and outside a circle, and [successively doubling the number of sides](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archimedes%27_doubling_method "Archimedes' doubling method") until he reached a 96-sided regular polygon. By calculating the perimeters of these polygons, he proved that ⁠223/71⁠ < π < ⁠22/7⁠ (that is, 3.1408 < π < 3.1429).[\[39\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-life-of-pi-42) Archimedes' upper bound of ⁠22/7⁠ may have led to a widespread popular belief that π is equal to ⁠22/7⁠.[\[40\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006171-43) Around 150AD, Greco-Roman scientist [Ptolemy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ptolemy "Ptolemy"), in his _[Almagest](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Almagest "Almagest")_, gave a value for π of 3.1416, which he may have obtained from Archimedes or from [Apollonius of Perga](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apollonius_of_Perga "Apollonius of Perga").[\[41\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006176-44)[\[42\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoyerMerzbach1991168-45)

In [ancient China](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ancient_China "Ancient China"), values for π included 3.1547 (around 1AD), 10{\\displaystyle {\\sqrt {10}}}![{\displaystyle {\sqrt {10}}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/dd7409b0ddbc1f90280265e7bc95dd20626ebf1b) (100AD, approximately 3.1623), and ⁠142/45⁠ (3rd century, approximately 3.1556).[\[43\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006176%E2%80%93177-46) Around 265AD, the [Cao Wei](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cao_Wei "Cao Wei") mathematician [Liu Hui](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Liu_Hui "Liu Hui") created a [polygon-based iterative algorithm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Liu_Hui%27s_%CF%80_algorithm "Liu Hui's π algorithm"), with which he constructed a 3,072-sided polygon to approximate π as3.1416.[\[44\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoyerMerzbach1991202-47)[\[45\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006177-48) Liu later invented a faster method of calculating π and obtained a value of 3.14 with a 96-sided polygon, by taking advantage of the fact that the differences in area of successive polygons form a geometric series with a factor of4.[\[44\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoyerMerzbach1991202-47) Around 480AD, [Zu Chongzhi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zu_Chongzhi "Zu Chongzhi") calculated that 3.1415926<π<3.1415927{\\displaystyle 3.1415926<\\pi <3.1415927}![{\displaystyle 3.1415926<\pi <3.1415927}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/2aca8712bcbd7836f7e1db9811a1e93ef71110aa) and suggested the approximations π≈355113=3.14159292035…{\\textstyle \\pi \\approx {\\frac {355}{113}}=3.14159292035\\ldots }![{\textstyle \pi \approx {\frac {355}{113}}=3.14159292035\ldots }](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/cd046d5d65d36945d4d32ae319152c6a251f3581) and π≈227=3.142857142857…{\\textstyle \\pi \\approx {\\frac {22}{7}}=3.142857142857\\ldots }![{\textstyle \pi \approx {\frac {22}{7}}=3.142857142857\ldots }](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4d80a8184e364d219d83e4ebfef8ecbd005606a6), which he termed the _[milü](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mil%C3%BC "Milü")_ ('close ratio') and _yuelü_ ('approximate ratio') respectively, iterating with Liu Hui's algorithm up to a 12,288-sided polygon. With a correct value for its seven first decimal digits, Zu's result remained the most accurate approximation of π for the next 800 years.[\[46\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006178-49)

The Indian astronomer [Aryabhata](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aryabhata "Aryabhata") used a value of 3.1416 in his _[Āryabhaṭīya](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/%C4%80ryabha%E1%B9%AD%C4%ABya "Āryabhaṭīya")_ (499AD).[\[47\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006179-50) Around 1220, [Fibonacci](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fibonacci "Fibonacci") computed 3.1418 using a polygonal method devised independently of Archimedes.[\[48\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006180-51) Italian author [Dante](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dante "Dante") apparently employed the value 3+210≈3.14142{\\textstyle 3+{\\frac {\\sqrt {2}}{10}}\\approx 3.14142}![{\textstyle 3+{\frac {\sqrt {2}}{10}}\approx 3.14142}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e7acb14f07f1be2393396e80d85e48abd0da0539).[\[48\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006180-51)

The Persian astronomer [Jamshīd al-Kāshī](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jamsh%C4%ABd_al-K%C4%81sh%C4%AB "Jamshīd al-Kāshī") produced nine [sexagesimal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexagesimal "Sexagesimal") digits, roughly the equivalent of 16 decimal digits, in 1424, using a polygon with 3×228{\\textstyle 3\\times 2^{28}}![{\textstyle 3\times 2^{28}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7e4f51cc91785a99c47ef48b6828cb4cf0020195) sides,[\[49\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-52) which stood as the world record for about 180 years.[\[50\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006182-53) French mathematician [François Viète](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Vi%C3%A8te "François Viète") in 1579 achieved nine digits with a polygon of 3×217{\\textstyle 3\\times 2^{17}}![{\textstyle 3\times 2^{17}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/21c6b820448824185aa6348247f55cc2490cceb9) sides.[\[50\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006182-53) Flemish mathematician [Adriaan van Roomen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Adriaan_van_Roomen "Adriaan van Roomen") arrived at 15 decimal places in 1593.[\[50\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006182-53) In 1596, Dutch mathematician [Ludolph van Ceulen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ludolph_van_Ceulen "Ludolph van Ceulen") reached 20 digits, a record he later increased to 35 digits (as a result, π was called the "Ludolphian number" in Germany until the early 20th century).[\[51\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006182%E2%80%93183-54) Dutch scientist [Willebrord Snellius](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Willebrord_Snellius "Willebrord Snellius") reached 34 digits in 1621,[\[52\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006183-55) and Austrian astronomer [Christoph Grienberger](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Christoph_Grienberger "Christoph Grienberger") arrived at 38 digits in 1630 using 1040 sides.[\[53\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-56) [Christiaan Huygens](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Christiaan_Huygens "Christiaan Huygens") was able to arrive at 10 decimal places in 1654 using a slightly different method equivalent to [Richardson extrapolation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Richardson_extrapolation "Richardson extrapolation").[\[54\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-57)

### Infinite series

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Comparison_pi_infinite_series.svg/330px-Comparison_pi_infinite_series.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Comparison_pi_infinite_series.svg) Comparison of the convergence of several historical infinite series for π. _Sn_ is the approximation after taking _n_ terms. Each subsequent subplot magnifies the shaded area horizontally by 10 times. [(click for detail)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Comparison_pi_infinite_series.svg "File:Comparison pi infinite series.svg")

The calculation of π was revolutionized by the development of [infinite series](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Infinite_series "Infinite series") techniques in the 16th and 17th centuries. An infinite series is the sum of the terms of an infinite [sequence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sequence_(mathematics) "Sequence (mathematics)"). Infinite series allowed mathematicians to compute π with much greater precision than [Archimedes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archimedes "Archimedes") and others who used geometrical techniques.[\[55\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006185%E2%80%93191-58) Although infinite series were exploited for π most notably by European mathematicians such as [James Gregory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/James_Gregory_(mathematician) "James Gregory (mathematician)") and [Gottfried Wilhelm Leibniz](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gottfried_Wilhelm_Leibniz "Gottfried Wilhelm Leibniz"), the approach also appeared in the [Kerala school](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kerala_school_of_astronomy_and_mathematics "Kerala school of astronomy and mathematics") sometime in the 14th or 15th century.[\[56\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-Roypp-59)[\[57\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006185%E2%80%93186-60) Around 1500, an infinite series that could be used to compute π, written in the form of [Sanskrit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sanskrit "Sanskrit") verse, was presented in _[Tantrasamgraha](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tantrasamgraha "Tantrasamgraha")_ by [Nilakantha Somayaji](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nilakantha_Somayaji "Nilakantha Somayaji").[\[56\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-Roypp-59) The series are presented without proof, but proofs are presented in the later work _[Yuktibhāṣā](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yuktibh%C4%81%E1%B9%A3%C4%81 "Yuktibhāṣā")_, published around 1530. Several infinite series are described, including series for sine (which Nilakantha attributes to [Madhava of Sangamagrama](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Madhava_of_Sangamagrama "Madhava of Sangamagrama")), cosine, and arctangent which are now sometimes referred to as [Madhava series](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Madhava_series "Madhava series"). The series for arctangent is sometimes called [Gregory's series](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gregory%27s_series "Gregory's series") or the Gregory–Leibniz series.[\[56\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-Roypp-59) Madhava used infinite series to estimate π to 11 digits around 1400.[\[58\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-61)[\[59\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAndrewsAskeyRoy199959-62)[\[60\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-63)

In 1593, [François Viète](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Vi%C3%A8te "François Viète") published what is now known as [Viète's formula](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vi%C3%A8te%27s_formula "Viète's formula"), an [infinite product](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Infinite_product "Infinite product") (rather than an [infinite sum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Infinite_sum "Infinite sum"), which is more typically used in π calculations):[\[61\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-64)

2π=22⋅2+22⋅2+2+22⋯{\\displaystyle {\\frac {2}{\\pi }}={\\frac {\\sqrt {2}}{2}}\\cdot {\\frac {\\sqrt {2+{\\sqrt {2}}}}{2}}\\cdot {\\frac {\\sqrt {2+{\\sqrt {2+{\\sqrt {2}}}}}}{2}}\\cdots }![{\displaystyle {\frac {2}{\pi }}={\frac {\sqrt {2}}{2}}\cdot {\frac {\sqrt {2+{\sqrt {2}}}}{2}}\cdot {\frac {\sqrt {2+{\sqrt {2+{\sqrt {2}}}}}}{2}}\cdots }](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/471259d8063f909988b7457a3e2d9c62f73efb01)

In 1655, [John Wallis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Wallis "John Wallis") published what is now known as the [Wallis product](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wallis_product "Wallis product"), also an infinite product:[\[62\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006187-65)

π2=(21⋅23)⋅(43⋅45)⋅(65⋅67)⋅(87⋅89)⋯{\\displaystyle {\\frac {\\pi }{2}}={\\Big (}{\\frac {2}{1}}\\cdot {\\frac {2}{3}}{\\Big )}\\cdot {\\Big (}{\\frac {4}{3}}\\cdot {\\frac {4}{5}}{\\Big )}\\cdot {\\Big (}{\\frac {6}{5}}\\cdot {\\frac {6}{7}}{\\Big )}\\cdot {\\Big (}{\\frac {8}{7}}\\cdot {\\frac {8}{9}}{\\Big )}\\cdots }![{\displaystyle {\frac {\pi }{2}}={\Big (}{\frac {2}{1}}\cdot {\frac {2}{3}}{\Big )}\cdot {\Big (}{\frac {4}{3}}\cdot {\frac {4}{5}}{\Big )}\cdot {\Big (}{\frac {6}{5}}\cdot {\frac {6}{7}}{\Big )}\cdot {\Big (}{\frac {8}{7}}\cdot {\frac {8}{9}}{\Big )}\cdots }](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c54735394beda8b94805a2919f1f4c0e200d2b29)

[![A formal portrait of a man, with long hair](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/GodfreyKneller-IsaacNewton-1689.jpg/250px-GodfreyKneller-IsaacNewton-1689.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:GodfreyKneller-IsaacNewton-1689.jpg) [Isaac Newton](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isaac_Newton "Isaac Newton") used [infinite series](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Infinite_series "Infinite series") to compute π to 15 digits, later writing "I am ashamed to tell you to how many figures I carried these computations".[\[63\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-Newton-66)

In the 1660s, the English scientist [Isaac Newton](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isaac_Newton "Isaac Newton") and German mathematician [Gottfried Wilhelm Leibniz](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gottfried_Wilhelm_Leibniz "Gottfried Wilhelm Leibniz") discovered [calculus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Calculus "Calculus"), which led to the development of many infinite series for approximating π. Newton himself used an arcsine series to compute a 15-digit approximation of π in 1665 or 1666, writing, "I am ashamed to tell you to how many figures I carried these computations, having no other business at the time."[\[63\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-Newton-66)

In 1671, [James Gregory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/James_Gregory_(mathematician) "James Gregory (mathematician)"), and independently, Leibniz in 1673, discovered the [Taylor series](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taylor_series "Taylor series") expansion for [arctangent](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arctangent "Arctangent"):[\[56\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-Roypp-59)[\[64\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-67)[\[65\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEymardLafon200453%E2%80%9354-68)

arctan⁡z=z−z33+z55−z77+⋯{\\displaystyle \\arctan z=z-{\\frac {z^{3}}{3}}+{\\frac {z^{5}}{5}}-{\\frac {z^{7}}{7}}+\\cdots }![{\displaystyle \arctan z=z-{\frac {z^{3}}{3}}+{\frac {z^{5}}{5}}-{\frac {z^{7}}{7}}+\cdots }](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ef0e80b93f9981701b98f8e121e27c4c50955a3d)

This series, sometimes called the [Gregory–Leibniz series](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gregory%27s_series "Gregory's series"), equals π4{\\textstyle {\\frac {\\pi }{4}}}![{\textstyle {\frac {\pi }{4}}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4cc79dd123359dbfd89513870537035bcbf0dd6d) when evaluated with ⁠z=1{\\displaystyle z=1}![{\displaystyle z=1}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/078535cde78d90bfa1d9fbb2446204593a921d57)⁠.[\[65\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEymardLafon200453%E2%80%9354-68) But for ⁠1{\\displaystyle {1}}![{\displaystyle {1}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9c209c2b7f722d78521ecf455dd33a01bd8e48e5)⁠, [it converges impractically slowly](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Leibniz_formula_for_%CF%80#Convergence "Leibniz formula for π") (that is, approaches the answer very gradually), taking about ten times as many terms to calculate each additional digit.[\[66\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-69)

In 1699, English mathematician [Abraham Sharp](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abraham_Sharp "Abraham Sharp") used the Gregory–Leibniz series for z=13{\\textstyle z={\\frac {1}{\\sqrt {3}}}}![{\textstyle z={\frac {1}{\sqrt {3}}}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4ae2125e0304d50f14a14f9559d3611557bbceaa) to compute π to 71 digits, breaking the previous record of 39 digits, which was set with a polygonal algorithm.[\[67\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006189-70)

In 1706, [John Machin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Machin "John Machin") used the Gregory–Leibniz series to produce an algorithm that converged much faster:[\[68\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-jones-71)[\[69\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-tweddle-72)[\[70\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006192%E2%80%93193-73)

π4=4arctan⁡15−arctan⁡1239.{\\displaystyle {\\frac {\\pi }{4}}=4\\arctan {\\frac {1}{5}}-\\arctan {\\frac {1}{239}}.}![{\displaystyle {\frac {\pi }{4}}=4\arctan {\frac {1}{5}}-\arctan {\frac {1}{239}}.}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d1e27dbc5ad6b84571aaaae627e1d0080145e6a1)

Machin reached 100 digits of π with this formula.[\[71\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel200672%E2%80%9374-74) Other mathematicians created variants, now known as [Machin-like formulae](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Machin-like_formula "Machin-like formula"), that were used to set several successive records for calculating digits of π.[\[72\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-75)[\[71\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel200672%E2%80%9374-74)

Isaac Newton [accelerated the convergence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Series_acceleration "Series acceleration") of the Gregory–Leibniz series in 1684 (in an unpublished work; others independently discovered the result):[\[73\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-76)

arctan⁡x=x1+x2+23x3(1+x2)2+2⋅43⋅5x5(1+x2)3+⋯{\\displaystyle \\arctan x={\\frac {x}{1+x^{2}}}+{\\frac {2}{3}}{\\frac {x^{3}}{(1+x^{2})^{2}}}+{\\frac {2\\cdot 4}{3\\cdot 5}}{\\frac {x^{5}}{(1+x^{2})^{3}}}+\\cdots }![{\displaystyle \arctan x={\frac {x}{1+x^{2}}}+{\frac {2}{3}}{\frac {x^{3}}{(1+x^{2})^{2}}}+{\frac {2\cdot 4}{3\cdot 5}}{\frac {x^{5}}{(1+x^{2})^{3}}}+\cdots }](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/6282b6fd3849a778a8897b3e40e25448e427a0bc)

[Leonhard Euler](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Leonhard_Euler "Leonhard Euler") popularized this series in his 1755 differential calculus textbook, and later used it with Machin-like formulae, including π4=5arctan⁡17+2arctan⁡379,{\\textstyle {\\tfrac {\\pi }{4}}=5\\arctan {\\tfrac {1}{7}}+2\\arctan {\\tfrac {3}{79}},}![{\textstyle {\tfrac {\pi }{4}}=5\arctan {\tfrac {1}{7}}+2\arctan {\tfrac {3}{79}},}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/202bbce8b648f8d1be219e9ea77aa0bc21230d08) with which he computed 20 digits of π in one hour.[\[74\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-77)

Machin-like formulae remained the best-known method for calculating π well into the age of computers, and were used to set records for 250 years, culminating in a 620-digit approximation in 1946 by Daniel Ferguson – the best approximation achieved without the aid of a calculating device.[\[75\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006192%E2%80%93196,_205-78)

In 1844, a record was set by [Zacharias Dase](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zacharias_Dase "Zacharias Dase"), who employed a Machin-like formula to calculate 200 decimals of π in his head at the behest of German mathematician [Carl Friedrich Gauss](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss "Carl Friedrich Gauss").[\[76\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006194%E2%80%93196-79)

In 1853, British mathematician [William Shanks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/William_Shanks "William Shanks") calculated π to 607 digits, but made a mistake in the 528th digit, rendering all subsequent digits incorrect. Though he calculated an additional 100 digits in 1873, bringing the total up to 707, his previous mistake rendered all the new digits incorrect as well.[\[77\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-hayes-2014-80)

#### Rate of convergence

Some infinite series for π [converge](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Convergent_series "Convergent series") faster than others. Given the choice of two infinite series for π, mathematicians will generally use the one that converges more rapidly because faster convergence reduces the amount of computation needed to calculate π to any given accuracy.[\[78\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-Aconverge-81) A simple infinite series for π is the [Gregory–Leibniz series](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Leibniz_formula_for_%CF%80 "Leibniz formula for π"):[\[79\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel200669%E2%80%9372-82)

π=41−43+45−47+49−411+413−⋯{\\displaystyle \\pi ={\\frac {4}{1}}-{\\frac {4}{3}}+{\\frac {4}{5}}-{\\frac {4}{7}}+{\\frac {4}{9}}-{\\frac {4}{11}}+{\\frac {4}{13}}-\\cdots }![{\displaystyle \pi ={\frac {4}{1}}-{\frac {4}{3}}+{\frac {4}{5}}-{\frac {4}{7}}+{\frac {4}{9}}-{\frac {4}{11}}+{\frac {4}{13}}-\cdots }](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e9e3959cd2d0ec735e7a6a1917df784842b76706)

As individual terms of this infinite series are added to the sum, the total gradually gets closer to π, and – with a sufficient number of terms – can get as close to π as desired. It converges quite slowly, though – after 500,000 terms, it produces only five correct decimal digits of π.[\[80\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-83)

An infinite series for π (published by Nilakantha in the 15th century) that converges more rapidly than the Gregory–Leibniz series is:[\[81\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006Formula_16.10,_p._223-84)[\[82\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-85)

π=3+42×3×4−44×5×6+46×7×8−48×9×10+⋯{\\displaystyle \\pi =3+{\\frac {4}{2\\times 3\\times 4}}-{\\frac {4}{4\\times 5\\times 6}}+{\\frac {4}{6\\times 7\\times 8}}-{\\frac {4}{8\\times 9\\times 10}}+\\cdots }![{\displaystyle \pi =3+{\frac {4}{2\times 3\times 4}}-{\frac {4}{4\times 5\times 6}}+{\frac {4}{6\times 7\times 8}}-{\frac {4}{8\times 9\times 10}}+\cdots }](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/fdafa8bd24ce2b6fd518a3cf253ad1ef409388a6)

The following table compares the convergence rates of these two series:

| Infinite series for π | After 1st term | After 2nd term | After 3rd term | After 4th term | After 5th term | Converges to: |
| --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
| π=41−43+45−47+49−411+413+⋯{\\displaystyle \\pi ={\\frac {4}{1}}-{\\frac {4}{3}}+{\\frac {4}{5}}-{\\frac {4}{7}}+{\\frac {4}{9}}-{\\frac {4}{11}}+{\\frac {4}{13}}+\\cdots }![{\displaystyle \pi ={\frac {4}{1}}-{\frac {4}{3}}+{\frac {4}{5}}-{\frac {4}{7}}+{\frac {4}{9}}-{\frac {4}{11}}+{\frac {4}{13}}+\cdots }](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/316235e9f73d29062935aefcb128b9be8060b5cb) | 4.0000 | 2.6666 ... | 3.4666 ... | 2.8952 ... | 3.3396 ... | π = 3.1415 ... |
| π=3+42×3×4−44×5×6+46×7×8−⋯{\\displaystyle \\pi ={3}+{\\frac {4}{2\\times 3\\times 4}}-{\\frac {4}{4\\times 5\\times 6}}+{\\frac {4}{6\\times 7\\times 8}}-\\cdots }![{\displaystyle \pi ={3}+{\frac {4}{2\times 3\times 4}}-{\frac {4}{4\times 5\times 6}}+{\frac {4}{6\times 7\times 8}}-\cdots }](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/758c0411cbd41eac7aef1ee7c9a46e84d64210b5) | 3.0000 | 3.1666 ... | 3.1333 ... | 3.1452 ... | 3.1396 ... |

After five terms, the sum of the Gregory–Leibniz series is within 0.2 of the correct value of π, whereas the sum of Nilakantha's series is within 0.002 of the correct value. Nilakantha's series converges faster and is more useful for computing digits of π. Series that converge even faster include [Machin's series](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Machin-like_formula "Machin-like formula") and [Chudnovsky's series](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chudnovsky_algorithm "Chudnovsky algorithm"), the latter producing 14 correct decimal digits per term.[\[78\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-Aconverge-81)

### Irrationality and transcendence

See also: [Proof that π is irrational](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proof_that_%CF%80_is_irrational "Proof that π is irrational") and [Proof that π is transcendental](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proof_that_%CF%80_is_transcendental "Proof that π is transcendental")

Not all mathematical advances relating to π were aimed at increasing the accuracy of approximations. When Euler solved the [Basel problem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Basel_problem "Basel problem") in 1735, finding the exact value of the sum of the reciprocal squares, he established a connection between π and the [prime numbers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number "Prime number") that later contributed to the development and study of the [Riemann zeta function](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Riemann_zeta_function "Riemann zeta function"):[\[83\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPosamentierLehmann2004284-86)

π26=112+122+132+142+⋯{\\displaystyle {\\frac {\\pi ^{2}}{6}}={\\frac {1}{1^{2}}}+{\\frac {1}{2^{2}}}+{\\frac {1}{3^{2}}}+{\\frac {1}{4^{2}}}+\\cdots }![{\displaystyle {\frac {\pi ^{2}}{6}}={\frac {1}{1^{2}}}+{\frac {1}{2^{2}}}+{\frac {1}{3^{2}}}+{\frac {1}{4^{2}}}+\cdots }](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/edf829fbf86ae73080bce0a95c497001b8d15fb1)

Swiss scientist [Johann Heinrich Lambert](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Johann_Heinrich_Lambert "Johann Heinrich Lambert") in 1768 proved that π is [irrational](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Irrational_number "Irrational number"), meaning it is not equal to the quotient of any two integers.[\[12\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel20065-13) [Lambert's proof](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proof_that_%CF%80_is_irrational "Proof that π is irrational") exploited a continued-fraction representation of the tangent function.[\[84\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-87) French mathematician [Adrien-Marie Legendre](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Adrien-Marie_Legendre "Adrien-Marie Legendre") proved in 1794 that π2 is also irrational. In 1882, German mathematician [Ferdinand von Lindemann](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ferdinand_von_Lindemann "Ferdinand von Lindemann") proved that π is [transcendental](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transcendental_number "Transcendental number"),[\[85\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-88) confirming a conjecture made by both [Legendre](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Adrien-Marie_Legendre "Adrien-Marie Legendre") and Euler.[\[86\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006196-89)[\[87\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-90)[\[d\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-91)

### Adoption of the symbol π

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/William_Jones%2C_the_Mathematician.jpg/250px-William_Jones%2C_the_Mathematician.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:William_Jones,_the_Mathematician.jpg)

The earliest known use of the Greek letter π to represent the ratio of a circle's circumference to its diameter was by Welsh mathematician [William Jones](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/William_Jones_(mathematician) "William Jones (mathematician)") in 1706.

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Leonhard_Euler.jpg/250px-Leonhard_Euler.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Leonhard_Euler.jpg)

[Leonhard Euler](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Leonhard_Euler "Leonhard Euler") popularized the use of the Greek letter π in works he published in 1736 and 1748.

The first recorded use of the symbol π in circle geometry is in [Oughtred's](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/William_Oughtred "William Oughtred") _Clavis Mathematicae_ (1648),[\[88\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-92)[\[89\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006166-93)
where the [Greek letters](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greek_letters "Greek letters") π and _δ_ were combined into the fraction ⁠πδ{\\displaystyle {\\tfrac {\\pi }{\\delta }}}![{\displaystyle {\tfrac {\pi }{\delta }}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/07042fa248fd5ceaffdd918e7291ab50ca86bf79)⁠ for denoting the ratios [semiperimeter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Semiperimeter "Semiperimeter") to [semidiameter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Semidiameter "Semidiameter") and perimeter to diameter, that is, what is presently denoted as π.[\[1\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-firstPi-1)[\[90\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-Cajori-2007-94)[\[91\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-Smith-1958-95)[\[92\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-96) (Before then, mathematicians sometimes used letters such as _c_ or _p_ instead.[\[89\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006166-93)) [Barrow](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isaac_Barrow "Isaac Barrow") likewise used the same notation,[\[93\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-97) while [Gregory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/David_Gregory_(mathematician) "David Gregory (mathematician)") instead used πρ{\\textstyle {\\frac {\\pi }{\\rho }}}![{\textstyle {\frac {\pi }{\rho }}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f62f00c9d88b9d6b7d4401227a9289982b4820a6) to represent 6.28... .[\[94\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-98)[\[91\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-Smith-1958-95)

The earliest known use of the Greek letter π alone to represent the ratio of a circle's circumference to its diameter was by Welsh mathematician [William Jones](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/William_Jones_(mathematician) "William Jones (mathematician)") in his 1706 work _Synopsis Palmariorum Matheseos; or, a New Introduction to the Mathematics_.[\[68\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-jones-71)[\[95\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006165-99) The Greek letter appears on p.243 in the phrase "12{\\textstyle {\\tfrac {1}{2}}}![{\textstyle {\tfrac {1}{2}}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/2558d30e006729499201220e98f83888914bef12) Periphery (π)", calculated for a circle with [radius](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radius "Radius") one. However, Jones writes that his equations for π are from the "ready pen of the truly ingenious Mr. [John Machin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Machin "John Machin")", leading to speculation that Machin may have employed the Greek letter before Jones.[\[89\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006166-93) Jones' notation was not immediately adopted by other mathematicians, with the fraction notation still being used as late as 1767.[\[90\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-Cajori-2007-94)[\[96\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-100)

[Euler](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Euler "Euler") started using the single-letter form beginning with his 1727 _Essay Explaining the Properties of Air_, though he used π = 6.28..., the ratio of periphery to radius, in this and some later writing.[\[97\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-101) Euler first used π = 3.14... in his 1736 work _[Mechanica](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mechanica "Mechanica")_,[\[98\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-102) and continued in his widely read 1748 work _[Introductio in analysin infinitorum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Introductio_in_analysin_infinitorum "Introductio in analysin infinitorum")_ (he wrote: "for the sake of brevity we will write this number as π; thus π is equal to half the circumference of a circle of radius 1").[\[99\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-103) Because Euler corresponded heavily with other mathematicians in Europe, the use of the Greek letter spread rapidly, and the practice was universally adopted thereafter in the [Western world](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Western_world "Western world"),[\[89\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006166-93) though the definition still varied between 3.14... and 6.28... as late as 1761.[\[100\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-104)

## Modern quest for more digits

### Motives for computing π

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Record_pi_approximations.svg/330px-Record_pi_approximations.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Record_pi_approximations.svg) As mathematicians discovered new algorithms, and computers became available, the number of known decimal digits of π increased dramatically. The vertical scale is [logarithmic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Logarithmic_scale "Logarithmic scale").

For most numerical calculations involving π, a handful of digits provide sufficient precision. According to Jörg Arndt and Christoph Haenel, thirty-nine digits are sufficient to perform most [cosmological](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cosmological "Cosmological") calculations, because that is the accuracy necessary to calculate the circumference of the [observable universe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Observable_universe "Observable universe") with a precision of one atom. Accounting for additional digits needed to compensate for computational [round-off errors](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Round-off_error "Round-off error"), Arndt concludes that a few hundred digits would suffice for any scientific application. Despite this, people have worked strenuously to compute π to thousands and millions of digits.[\[101\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel200617%E2%80%9319-105) This effort may be partly ascribed to the human compulsion to break records, and such achievements with π often make headlines around the world.[\[102\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-106) They also have practical benefits, such as testing [supercomputers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Supercomputer "Supercomputer"), testing numerical analysis algorithms (including [high-precision multiplication algorithms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Multiplication_algorithm#Fast_multiplication_algorithms_for_large_inputs "Multiplication algorithm")) – and within pure mathematics itself, providing data for evaluating the randomness of the digits of π.[\[103\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel200617%E2%80%9318-107)[\[104\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-108)

### Computer era and iterative algorithms

The development of computers in the mid-20th century again revolutionized the hunt for digits of π. Mathematicians [John Wrench](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Wrench "John Wrench") and Levi Smith reached 1,120 digits in 1949 using a desk calculator.[\[105\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006205-109) Using an [inverse tangent](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inverse_tangent "Inverse tangent") (arctan) infinite series, a team led by George Reitwiesner and [John von Neumann](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann "John von Neumann") that same year achieved 2,037 digits with a calculation that took 70 hours of computer time on the [ENIAC](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ENIAC "ENIAC") computer.[\[106\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006197-110)[\[107\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-111) The record, always relying on an arctan series, was broken repeatedly (3,089 digits in 1955,[\[108\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-112) 7,480 digits in 1957; 10,000 digits in 1958; 100,000 digits in 1961) until 1 million digits was reached in 1973.[\[106\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006197-110)

Two additional developments around 1980 once again accelerated the ability to compute π. First, the discovery of new [iterative algorithms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iterative_algorithm "Iterative algorithm") for computing π, which were much faster than the infinite series; and second, the invention of [fast multiplication algorithms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Multiplication_algorithm#Fast_multiplication_algorithms_for_large_inputs "Multiplication algorithm") that could multiply large numbers very rapidly.[\[109\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel200615%E2%80%9317-113) Such algorithms are particularly important in modern π computations because most of the computer's time is devoted to multiplication.[\[110\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006131-114) They include the [Karatsuba algorithm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karatsuba_algorithm "Karatsuba algorithm"), [Toom–Cook multiplication](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Toom%E2%80%93Cook_multiplication "Toom–Cook multiplication"), and [Fourier transform-based methods](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/FFT_multiplication#Fourier_transform_methods "FFT multiplication").[\[111\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006132,_140-115)

> The [Gauss–Legendre iterative algorithm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gauss%E2%80%93Legendre_algorithm "Gauss–Legendre algorithm"):
>
> Initialize
> a0=1,b0=12,t0=14,p0=1.{\\displaystyle \\textstyle a\_{0}=1,\\quad b\_{0}={\\frac {1}{\\sqrt {2}}},\\quad t\_{0}={\\frac {1}{4}},\\quad p\_{0}=1.}![{\displaystyle \textstyle a_{0}=1,\quad b_{0}={\frac {1}{\sqrt {2}}},\quad t_{0}={\frac {1}{4}},\quad p_{0}=1.}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/289b9555fe4184d8c18ce84e6a20078fcf53cc07)
> Iterate
> an+1=an+bn2,bn+1=anbn,{\\displaystyle \\textstyle a\_{n+1}={\\frac {a\_{n}+b\_{n}}{2}},\\quad \\quad b\_{n+1}={\\sqrt {a\_{n}b\_{n}}},}![{\displaystyle \textstyle a_{n+1}={\frac {a_{n}+b_{n}}{2}},\quad \quad b_{n+1}={\sqrt {a_{n}b_{n}}},}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d1078754ad2314049f7046646f743377e6aaf0c2)tn+1=tn−pn(an−an+1)2,pn+1=2pn.{\\displaystyle \\textstyle t\_{n+1}=t\_{n}-p\_{n}(a\_{n}-a\_{n+1})^{2},\\quad \\quad p\_{n+1}=2p\_{n}.}![{\displaystyle \textstyle t_{n+1}=t_{n}-p_{n}(a_{n}-a_{n+1})^{2},\quad \quad p_{n+1}=2p_{n}.}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ab091570d1e7c23425a7c86b021c310ec7f489ec)
> Then an estimate for π is given by
> π≈(an+bn)24tn.{\\displaystyle \\textstyle \\pi \\approx {\\frac {(a\_{n}+b\_{n})^{2}}{4t\_{n}}}.}![{\displaystyle \textstyle \pi \approx {\frac {(a_{n}+b_{n})^{2}}{4t_{n}}}.}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ac6a29dbd699744dac1aa6c8e615551a94c25b07)

The iterative algorithms were independently published in 1975–1976 by physicist [Eugene Salamin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eugene_Salamin_(mathematician) "Eugene Salamin (mathematician)") and scientist [Richard Brent](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Richard_Brent_(scientist) "Richard Brent (scientist)").[\[112\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel200687-116) These avoid reliance on infinite series. An iterative algorithm repeats a specific calculation, each iteration using the outputs from prior steps as its inputs, and produces a result in each step that converges to the desired value. The approach was actually invented over 160 years earlier by [Carl Friedrich Gauss](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss "Carl Friedrich Gauss"), in what is now termed the [arithmetic–geometric mean method](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/AGM_method "AGM method") (AGM method) or [Gauss–Legendre algorithm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gauss%E2%80%93Legendre_algorithm "Gauss–Legendre algorithm").[\[112\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel200687-116) As modified by Salamin and Brent, it is also referred to as the Brent–Salamin algorithm.

The iterative algorithms were widely used after 1980 because they are faster than infinite series algorithms: whereas infinite series typically increase the number of correct digits additively in successive terms, iterative algorithms generally _multiply_ the number of correct digits at each step. For example, the Brent–Salamin algorithm doubles the number of digits in each iteration. In 1984, brothers [John](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jonathan_Borwein "Jonathan Borwein") and [Peter Borwein](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peter_Borwein "Peter Borwein") produced an iterative algorithm that quadruples the number of digits in each step; and in 1987, one that increases the number of digits five times in each step.[\[113\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-117) Iterative methods were used by Japanese mathematician [Yasumasa Kanada](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yasumasa_Kanada "Yasumasa Kanada") to set several records for computing π between 1995 and 2002.[\[114\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-Background-118) This rapid convergence comes at a price: the iterative algorithms require significantly more [memory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Computer_memory "Computer memory") than infinite series.[\[114\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-Background-118)

### Rapidly convergent series

[![Photo portrait of a man](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Srinivasa_Ramanujan_-_OPC_-_2_%28cleaned%29.jpg/250px-Srinivasa_Ramanujan_-_OPC_-_2_%28cleaned%29.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Srinivasa_Ramanujan_-_OPC_-_2_(cleaned).jpg) [Srinivasa Ramanujan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Srinivasa_Ramanujan "Srinivasa Ramanujan"), working in isolation in India, produced many innovative series for computing π.

Modern π calculators do not use iterative algorithms exclusively. New infinite series were discovered in the 1980s and 1990s that are as fast as iterative algorithms, yet are simpler and less memory intensive.[\[114\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-Background-118) The fast iterative algorithms were anticipated in 1914, when Indian mathematician [Srinivasa Ramanujan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Srinivasa_Ramanujan "Srinivasa Ramanujan") published dozens of innovative new formulae for π, remarkable for their elegance, mathematical depth and rapid convergence.[\[115\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006103%E2%80%93104-119) One of his formulae, based on [modular equations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Modular_equation "Modular equation"), is
