1π=229801∑k=0∞(4k)!(1103+26390k)k!43964k.{\\displaystyle {\\frac {1}{\\pi }}={\\frac {2{\\sqrt {2}}}{9801}}\\sum \_{k=0}^{\\infty }{\\frac {(4k)!(1103+26390k)}{k!^{4}396^{4k}}}.}![{\displaystyle {\frac {1}{\pi }}={\frac {2{\sqrt {2}}}{9801}}\sum _{k=0}^{\infty }{\frac {(4k)!(1103+26390k)}{k!^{4}396^{4k}}}.}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c1526bfafdfc0417cd5ab8afc95f8d2d28d09259)

This series converges much more rapidly than most arctan series, including Machin's formula.[\[116\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006104-120) [Bill Gosper](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bill_Gosper "Bill Gosper") was the first to use it for advances in the calculation of π, setting a record of 17 million digits in 1985.[\[117\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006104,_206-121) Ramanujan's formulae anticipated the modern algorithms developed by the Borwein brothers ( [Jonathan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jonathan_Borwein "Jonathan Borwein") and [Peter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peter_Borwein "Peter Borwein")) and the [Chudnovsky brothers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chudnovsky_brothers "Chudnovsky brothers").[\[118\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006110%E2%80%93111-122) The [Chudnovsky formula](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chudnovsky_algorithm "Chudnovsky algorithm") developed in 1987 is

1π=100054270934400∑k=0∞(6k)!(13591409+545140134k)(3k)!k!3(−640320)3k.{\\displaystyle {\\frac {1}{\\pi }}={\\frac {\\sqrt {10005}}{4270934400}}\\sum \_{k=0}^{\\infty }{\\frac {(6k)!(13591409+545140134k)}{(3k)!\\,k!^{3}(-640320)^{3k}}}.}![{\displaystyle {\frac {1}{\pi }}={\frac {\sqrt {10005}}{4270934400}}\sum _{k=0}^{\infty }{\frac {(6k)!(13591409+545140134k)}{(3k)!\,k!^{3}(-640320)^{3k}}}.}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/79ab7f20d9e679a0ab580eb0d1e63bfd7ea0767a)

It produces about 14 digits of π per term[\[119\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEymardLafon2004254-123) and has been used for several record-setting π calculations, including the first to surpass 1 billion (109) digits in 1989 by the Chudnovsky brothers, 10 trillion (1013) digits in 2011 by Alexander Yee and Shigeru Kondo,[\[120\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-NW-124) and 100 trillion digits by [Emma Haruka Iwao](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Emma_Haruka_Iwao "Emma Haruka Iwao") in 2022.[\[121\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-125)[\[122\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-126) For similar formulae, see also the [Ramanujan–Sato series](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ramanujan%E2%80%93Sato_series "Ramanujan–Sato series").

In 2006, mathematician [Simon Plouffe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Simon_Plouffe "Simon Plouffe") used the PSLQ [integer relation algorithm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Integer_relation_algorithm "Integer relation algorithm")[\[123\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-127) to generate several new formulae for π, conforming to the following template:

πk=∑n=1∞1nk(aqn−1+bq2n−1+cq4n−1),{\\displaystyle \\pi ^{k}=\\sum \_{n=1}^{\\infty }{\\frac {1}{n^{k}}}\\left({\\frac {a}{q^{n}-1}}+{\\frac {b}{q^{2n}-1}}+{\\frac {c}{q^{4n}-1}}\\right),}![{\displaystyle \pi ^{k}=\sum _{n=1}^{\infty }{\frac {1}{n^{k}}}\left({\frac {a}{q^{n}-1}}+{\frac {b}{q^{2n}-1}}+{\frac {c}{q^{4n}-1}}\right),}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/948b735a39a1bbf9a39ba0b6bd7dc63bddcd4351)

where q is [_e_ _π_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gelfond%27s_constant "Gelfond's constant") (Gelfond's constant), k is an [odd number](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Odd_number "Odd number"), and a, b, c are certain rational numbers that Plouffe computed.[\[124\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-128)

### Monte Carlo methods

[![Needles of length ℓ scattered on stripes with width t](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Buffon_needle.svg/330px-Buffon_needle.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Buffon_needle.svg)

[Buffon's needle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Buffon%27s_needle "Buffon's needle"). Needles _a_ and _b_ are dropped randomly.

[![Thousands of dots randomly covering a square and a circle inscribed in the square.](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Pi_30K.gif/250px-Pi_30K.gif)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Pi_30K.gif)

Random dots are placed on a square and a circle inscribed inside.

[Monte Carlo methods](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Monte_Carlo_methods "Monte Carlo methods"), which evaluate the results of multiple random trials, can be used to create approximations of π.[\[125\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel200639-129) [Buffon's needle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Buffon%27s_needle "Buffon's needle") is one such technique: If a needle of length ℓ is dropped n times on a surface on which parallel lines are drawn t units apart, and if x of those times it comes to rest crossing a line (_x_ \> 0), then one may approximate π based on the counts:[\[126\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-bn-130)

π≈2nℓxt.{\\displaystyle \\pi \\approx {\\frac {2n\\ell }{xt}}.}![{\displaystyle \pi \approx {\frac {2n\ell }{xt}}.}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/6e72a1af2e84df27e3b491d5ffcf140960d0b3af)

Another Monte Carlo method for computing π is to draw a circle inscribed in a square, and randomly place dots in the square. The ratio of dots inside the circle to the total number of dots will approximately equal _π_/4.[\[127\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-131)

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e4/Five_random_walks.png/250px-Five_random_walks.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Five_random_walks.png) Five random walks with 200 steps. The sample mean of \|_W_ 200\| is _μ_ = 56/5, and so 2(200) _μ_ −2 ≈ 3.19 is within 0.05 ofπ.

Another way to calculate π using probability is to start with a [random walk](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Random_walk "Random walk"), generated by a sequence of (fair) coin tosses: independent [random variables](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Random_variable "Random variable") _Xk_ such that _Xk_ ∈ {−1,1} with equal probabilities. The associated random walk is

Wn=∑k=1nXk{\\displaystyle W\_{n}=\\sum \_{k=1}^{n}X\_{k}}![{\displaystyle W_{n}=\sum _{k=1}^{n}X_{k}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ff50676493705f91fc45cb5ab160083bbf7aa669)

so that, for each n, _Wn_ is drawn from a shifted and scaled [binomial distribution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Binomial_distribution "Binomial distribution"). As n varies, _Wn_ defines a (discrete) [stochastic process](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stochastic_process "Stochastic process"). Then π can be calculated by[\[128\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-132)

π=limn→∞2nE\[\|Wn\|\]2.{\\displaystyle \\pi =\\lim \_{n\\to \\infty }{\\frac {2n}{E\[\|W\_{n}\|\]^{2}}}.}![{\displaystyle \pi =\lim _{n\to \infty }{\frac {2n}{E[|W_{n}|]^{2}}}.}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a691be63815c6b7d9fe15070ae98039d9c1d0384)

This Monte Carlo method is independent of any relation to circles, and is a consequence of the [central limit theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Central_limit_theorem "Central limit theorem"), discussed [below](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#Gaussian_integrals).

These Monte Carlo methods for approximating π are very slow compared to other methods, and do not provide any information on the exact number of digits obtained. Thus they are never used to approximate π when speed or accuracy is desired.[\[129\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-133)

### Spigot algorithms

Two algorithms were discovered in 1995 that opened up new avenues of research into π. They are called [spigot algorithms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spigot_algorithm "Spigot algorithm") because, like water dripping from a [spigot](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tap_(valve) "Tap (valve)"), they produce single digits of π that are not reused after they are calculated.[\[130\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel200677%E2%80%9384-134)[\[131\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-Gibbons-135) This is in contrast to infinite series or iterative algorithms, which retain and use all intermediate digits until the final result is produced.[\[130\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel200677%E2%80%9384-134)

Mathematicians [Stan Wagon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stan_Wagon "Stan Wagon") and Stanley Rabinowitz produced a simple spigot algorithm in 1995.[\[131\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-Gibbons-135)[\[132\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel200677-136)[\[133\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-137) Its speed is comparable to arctan algorithms, but not as fast as iterative algorithms.[\[132\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel200677-136)

Another spigot algorithm, the [BBP](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bailey%E2%80%93Borwein%E2%80%93Plouffe_formula "Bailey–Borwein–Plouffe formula") [digit extraction algorithm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Digit_extraction_algorithm "Digit extraction algorithm"), was discovered in 1995 by Simon Plouffe:[\[134\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006117,_126%E2%80%93128-138)[\[135\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-bbpf-139)π=∑k=0∞116k(48k+1−28k+4−18k+5−18k+6).{\\displaystyle \\pi =\\sum \_{k=0}^{\\infty }{\\frac {1}{16^{k}}}\\left({\\frac {4}{8k+1}}-{\\frac {2}{8k+4}}-{\\frac {1}{8k+5}}-{\\frac {1}{8k+6}}\\right).}![{\displaystyle \pi =\sum _{k=0}^{\infty }{\frac {1}{16^{k}}}\left({\frac {4}{8k+1}}-{\frac {2}{8k+4}}-{\frac {1}{8k+5}}-{\frac {1}{8k+6}}\right).}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c092c788f1ff39174a3f78c5a329d5aa83ca2add)

This formula, unlike others before it, can produce any individual [hexadecimal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hexadecimal "Hexadecimal") digit of π without calculating all the preceding digits.[\[134\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel2006117,_126%E2%80%93128-138) Individual binary digits may be extracted from individual hexadecimal digits, and [octal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Octal "Octal") digits can be extracted from one or two hexadecimal digits. An important application of digit extraction algorithms is to validate new claims of record π computations: After a new record is claimed, the decimal result is converted to hexadecimal, and then a digit extraction algorithm is used to calculate several randomly selected hexadecimal digits near the end; if they match, this provides a measure of confidence that the entire computation is correct.[\[120\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-NW-124)

Between 1998 and 2000, the [distributed computing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Distributed_computing "Distributed computing") project [PiHex](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/PiHex "PiHex") used [Bellard's formula](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bellard%27s_formula "Bellard's formula") (a modification of the BBP algorithm) to compute the quadrillionth (1015th) bit of π, which turned out to be 0.[\[136\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-140) In September 2010, a [Yahoo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yahoo "Yahoo") employee used the company's [Hadoop](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apache_Hadoop "Apache Hadoop") application on one thousand computers over a 23-day period to compute 256 [bits](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bit "Bit") of π at the two-quadrillionth (2×1015th) bit, which also happens to be zero.[\[137\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-141)

In 2022, Plouffe found a base-10 algorithm for calculating digits of π.[\[138\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-142)

## Role and characterizations in mathematics

Because π is closely related to the circle, it is found in [many formulae](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_formulae_involving_%CF%80 "List of formulae involving π") from the fields of geometry and trigonometry, particularly those concerning circles, spheres, or ellipses. Other branches of science, such as statistics, physics, [Fourier analysis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fourier_analysis "Fourier analysis"), and number theory, also include π in some of their important formulae.

### Geometry and trigonometry

[![A diagram of a circle with a square coving the circle's upper right quadrant](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Circle_Area.svg/250px-Circle_Area.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Circle_Area.svg) The area of the circle equals π times the shaded area. The area of the [unit circle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Unit_circle "Unit circle") is π.

π appears in formulae for areas and volumes of geometrical shapes based on circles, such as [ellipses](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ellipse "Ellipse"), [spheres](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sphere "Sphere"), [cones](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cone_(geometry) "Cone (geometry)"), and [tori](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Torus "Torus").[\[139\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-143) Below are some of the more common formulae that involve π.[\[140\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-144)

- The circumference of a circle with radius r is 2 _πr_.[\[141\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-145)
- The [area of a circle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Area_of_a_disk "Area of a disk") with radius r is _πr_ 2.
- The area of an ellipse with semi-major axis a and semi-minor axis b is _πab_.[\[142\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-146)
- The volume of a sphere with radius r is ⁠4/3⁠ _πr_ 3.
- The surface area of a sphere with radius r is 4 _πr_ 2.

Some of the formulae above are special cases of the volume of the [_n_-dimensional ball](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/N-ball "N-ball") and the surface area of its boundary, the [( _n_ −1)-dimensional sphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/N-sphere "N-sphere"), given [below](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#The_gamma_function_and_Stirling's_approximation).

Apart from circles, there are other [curves of constant width](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Curve_of_constant_width "Curve of constant width"). By [Barbier's theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barbier%27s_theorem "Barbier's theorem"), every curve of constant width has perimeter π times its width. The [Reuleaux triangle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reuleaux_triangle "Reuleaux triangle") (formed by the intersection of three circles with the sides of an [equilateral triangle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Equilateral_triangle "Equilateral triangle") as their radii) has the smallest possible area for its width and the circle the largest. There also exist non-circular [smooth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Smoothness "Smoothness") and even [algebraic curves](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Algebraic_curve "Algebraic curve") of constant width.[\[143\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-147)

[Definite integrals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Integral "Integral") that describe circumference, area, or volume of shapes generated by circles typically have values that involve π. For example, an integral that specifies half the area of a circle of radius one is given by:[\[144\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-148)

∫−111−x2dx=π2.{\\displaystyle \\int \_{-1}^{1}{\\sqrt {1-x^{2}}}\\,dx={\\frac {\\pi }{2}}.}![{\displaystyle \int _{-1}^{1}{\sqrt {1-x^{2}}}\,dx={\frac {\pi }{2}}.}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/1955a15a053adcb91a83af56ea651085c2c18353)

In that integral, the function 1−x2{\\displaystyle {\\sqrt {1-x^{2}}}}![{\displaystyle {\sqrt {1-x^{2}}}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/875fba356d7f4befcacf6573012100b3ef95cd19) represents the height over the x{\\displaystyle x}![{\displaystyle x}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/87f9e315fd7e2ba406057a97300593c4802b53e4)-axis of a [semicircle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Semicircle "Semicircle") (the [square root](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Square_root "Square root") is a consequence of the [Pythagorean theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pythagorean_theorem "Pythagorean theorem")), and the integral computes the area below the semicircle. The existence of such integrals makes π an [algebraic period](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Period_(algebraic_geometry) "Period (algebraic geometry)").[\[145\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-149)

### Unit of angle

See also: [Units of angle measure](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Units_of_angle_measure "Units of angle measure")

[![Diagram showing graphs of functions](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Sine_cosine_one_period.svg/500px-Sine_cosine_one_period.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Sine_cosine_one_period.svg) [Sine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sine "Sine") and [cosine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cosine "Cosine") functions repeat with period 2π.

The [trigonometric functions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trigonometric_functions "Trigonometric functions") rely on [angles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Angle "Angle"), and mathematicians generally use the [radian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radian "Radian") as a unit of measurement. π plays an important role in angles measured in radians, which are defined so that a complete circle spans an angle of 2πradians. The angle measure of 180° is equal to πradians, and 1° = π/180 radians.[\[146\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAbramson2014[httpsopenstaxorgbooksprecalculuspages5-1-angles_Section_5.1:_Angles]-150)

Common trigonometric functions have periods that are multiples of π; for example, sine and cosine have period 2π, so for any angle θ and any integer k,[\[147\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBronshte%C4%ADnSemendiaev1971210%E2%80%93211-151)sin⁡θ=sin⁡(θ+2πk) and cos⁡θ=cos⁡(θ+2πk).{\\displaystyle \\sin \\theta =\\sin \\left(\\theta +2\\pi k\\right){\\text{ and }}\\cos \\theta =\\cos \\left(\\theta +2\\pi k\\right).}![{\displaystyle \sin \theta =\sin \left(\theta +2\pi k\right){\text{ and }}\cos \theta =\cos \left(\theta +2\pi k\right).}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f3b3c88ab8201f4f0c6f18dd73cb951ebcd07e2a)

### Eigenvalues

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Harmonic_partials_on_strings.svg/250px-Harmonic_partials_on_strings.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Harmonic_partials_on_strings.svg) The [overtones](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Overtone "Overtone") of a vibrating string are [eigenfunctions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eigenfunction "Eigenfunction") of the second derivative, and form a [harmonic progression](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Harmonic_series_(music) "Harmonic series (music)"). The associated eigenvalues form the [arithmetic progression](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arithmetic_progression "Arithmetic progression") of integer multiples of π.

Many of the appearances of π in the formulae of mathematics and the sciences have to do with its close relationship with geometry. However, π also appears in many natural situations having apparently nothing to do with geometry.

In many applications, it plays a distinguished role as an [eigenvalue](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eigenvalue "Eigenvalue"). For example, an idealized [vibrating string](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vibrating_string "Vibrating string") can be modelled as the graph of a function f on the unit interval \[0, 1\], with [fixed ends](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Boundary_conditions "Boundary conditions") _f_(0) = _f_(1) = 0. The modes of vibration of the string are solutions of the [differential equation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Differential_equation "Differential equation") ⁠f″(x)+λf(x)=0{\\displaystyle f''(x)+\\lambda f(x)=0}![{\displaystyle f''(x)+\lambda f(x)=0}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/dd94cf1cb3f339145da51a6494cf8ea95c5644c6)⁠, or ⁠f″(t)=−λf(x){\\displaystyle f''(t)=-\\lambda f(x)}![{\displaystyle f''(t)=-\lambda f(x)}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/36bfe6a78ecda33af55f241344a0ce2cc5862794)⁠. Thus λ is an eigenvalue of the second derivative [operator](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Differential_operator "Differential operator") ⁠f↦f″{\\displaystyle f\\mapsto f''}![{\displaystyle f\mapsto f''}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/8d92f8b043c42cc84705fe283f2e325b8df6b20a)⁠, and is constrained by [Sturm–Liouville theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sturm%E2%80%93Liouville_theory "Sturm–Liouville theory") to take on only certain specific values. It must be positive, since the operator is [negative definite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Negative_definite "Negative definite"), so it is convenient to write _λ_ = _ν_ 2, where _ν_ \> 0 is called the [wavenumber](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wavenumber "Wavenumber"). Then _f_( _x_) = sin( _π_ _x_) satisfies the boundary conditions and the differential equation with _ν_ = _π_.[\[148\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-152)

The value π is, in fact, the _least_ such value of the wavenumber, and is associated with the [fundamental mode](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fundamental_mode "Fundamental mode") of vibration of the string. One way to show this is by estimating the [energy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Energy "Energy"), which satisfies [Wirtinger's inequality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wirtinger%27s_inequality_for_functions "Wirtinger's inequality for functions"):[\[149\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDymMcKean197247-153) for a function f:\[0,1\]→C{\\displaystyle f:\[0,1\]\\to \\mathbb {C} }![{\displaystyle f:[0,1]\to \mathbb {C} }](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f0468f15485d405d64092878cda0fc0cbdab2f62) with _f_(0) = _f_(1) = 0 and f, _f_′ both [square integrable](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Square_integrable "Square integrable"), we have:

π2∫01\|f(x)\|2dx≤∫01\|f′(x)\|2dx,{\\displaystyle \\pi ^{2}\\int \_{0}^{1}\|f(x)\|^{2}\\,dx\\leq \\int \_{0}^{1}\|f'(x)\|^{2}\\,dx,}![{\displaystyle \pi ^{2}\int _{0}^{1}|f(x)|^{2}\,dx\leq \int _{0}^{1}|f'(x)|^{2}\,dx,}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9021233d9bdca65d39dc801347f5901f04e55204)

with equality precisely when f is a multiple of sin( _π_ _x_). Here π appears as an optimal constant in Wirtinger's inequality, and it follows that it is the smallest wavenumber, using the [variational characterization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Variational_theorem "Variational theorem") of the eigenvalue. As a consequence, π is the smallest [singular value](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Singular_value "Singular value") of the derivative operator on the space of functions on \[0, 1\] vanishing at both endpoints (the [Sobolev space](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sobolev_space "Sobolev space")H01\[0,1\]{\\displaystyle H\_{0}^{1}\[0,1\]}![{\displaystyle H_{0}^{1}[0,1]}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7113d194de39d54621e9da47782ad5263b9f1790)).

### Inequalities

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Sir_William_Thompson_illustration_of_Carthage.png/250px-Sir_William_Thompson_illustration_of_Carthage.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Sir_William_Thompson_illustration_of_Carthage.png) The [ancient city of Carthage](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ancient_Carthage "Ancient Carthage") was the solution to an isoperimetric problem, according to a legend recounted by [Lord Kelvin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lord_Kelvin "Lord Kelvin"):[\[150\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-154) those lands bordering the sea that [Queen Dido](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dido "Dido") could enclose on all other sides within a single given oxhide, cut into strips.

The number π serves appears in similar eigenvalue problems in higher-dimensional analysis. As mentioned [above](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#Definition), it can be characterized via its role as the best constant in the [isoperimetric inequality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isoperimetric_inequality "Isoperimetric inequality"): the area A enclosed by a plane [Jordan curve](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jordan_curve "Jordan curve") of perimeter P satisfies the inequality

4πA≤P2,{\\displaystyle 4\\pi A\\leq P^{2},}![{\displaystyle 4\pi A\leq P^{2},}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/2da151eb3d06c6538dfa721d85c36e2b052a5114)

and equality is clearly achieved for the circle, since in that case _A_ = _πr_ 2 and _P_ = 2 _πr_.[\[151\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-155)

Ultimately, as a consequence of the isoperimetric inequality, π appears in the optimal constant for the critical [Sobolev inequality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sobolev_inequality "Sobolev inequality") in _n_ dimensions, which thus characterizes the role of π in many physical phenomena as well, for example those of classical [potential theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Potential_theory "Potential theory").[\[152\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-156)[\[153\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-157)[\[154\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-158) In two dimensions, the critical Sobolev inequality is

2π‖f‖2≤‖∇f‖1{\\displaystyle 2\\pi \\\|f\\\|\_{2}\\leq \\\|\\nabla f\\\|\_{1}}![{\displaystyle 2\pi \|f\|_{2}\leq \|\nabla f\|_{1}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a80eaaf07fddda0c919000c07fda359208e10cf9)

for _f_ a smooth function with compact support in **R** 2, ∇f{\\displaystyle \\nabla f}![{\displaystyle \nabla f}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b7b4d6de89b52c5a5e6e1583cb63eaee263e307b) is the [gradient](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gradient "Gradient") of _f_, and ‖f‖2{\\displaystyle \\\|f\\\|\_{2}}![{\displaystyle \|f\|_{2}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7d0c64fd6ef8f74eac7ace4d9b3db3f4d148385d) and ‖∇f‖1{\\displaystyle \\\|\\nabla f\\\|\_{1}}![{\displaystyle \|\nabla f\|_{1}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7de8dc3d0605c979ebae27c6791e3df7b1c19887) refer respectively to the [L2 and L1-norm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lp_space "Lp space"). The Sobolev inequality is equivalent to the isoperimetric inequality (in any dimension), with the same best constants.

Wirtinger's inequality also generalizes to higher-dimensional [Poincaré inequalities](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Poincar%C3%A9_inequality "Poincaré inequality") that provide best constants for the [Dirichlet energy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dirichlet_energy "Dirichlet energy") of an _n_-dimensional membrane. Specifically, π is the greatest constant such that

π≤(∫G\|∇u\|2)1/2(∫G\|u\|2)1/2{\\displaystyle \\pi \\leq {\\frac {\\left(\\int \_{G}\|\\nabla u\|^{2}\\right)^{1/2}}{\\left(\\int \_{G}\|u\|^{2}\\right)^{1/2}}}}![{\displaystyle \pi \leq {\frac {\left(\int _{G}|\nabla u|^{2}\right)^{1/2}}{\left(\int _{G}|u|^{2}\right)^{1/2}}}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/72b72bae66777c4041fd32d69be2a43a740bd885)

for all [convex](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Convex_set "Convex set") subsets G of **R** _n_ of diameter 1, and square-integrable functions _u_ on G of mean zero.[\[155\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-159) Just as Wirtinger's inequality is the [variational](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Calculus_of_variations "Calculus of variations") form of the [Dirichlet eigenvalue](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dirichlet_eigenvalue "Dirichlet eigenvalue") problem in one dimension, the Poincaré inequality is the variational form of the [Neumann](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neumann_problem "Neumann problem") eigenvalue problem, in any dimension.

### Fourier transform and Heisenberg uncertainty principle

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Animation_of_Heisenberg_geodesic.gif/250px-Animation_of_Heisenberg_geodesic.gif)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Animation_of_Heisenberg_geodesic.gif) An animation of a [geodesic in the Heisenberg group](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heisenberg_group#As_a_sub-Riemannian_manifold "Heisenberg group")

The constant π also appears as a critical spectral parameter in the [Fourier transform](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fourier_transform "Fourier transform"). This is the [integral transform](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Integral_transform "Integral transform"), that takes a complex-valued integrable function f on the real line to the function defined as:

f^(ξ)=∫−∞∞f(x)e−2πixξdx.{\\displaystyle {\\hat {f}}(\\xi )=\\int \_{-\\infty }^{\\infty }f(x)e^{-2\\pi ix\\xi }\\,dx.}![{\displaystyle {\hat {f}}(\xi )=\int _{-\infty }^{\infty }f(x)e^{-2\pi ix\xi }\,dx.}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4a78cc05a0945443f61906a7c66822e38839e84f)

Although there are several different conventions for the Fourier transform and its inverse, any such convention must involve π _somewhere_. The above is the most canonical definition, however, giving the unique unitary operator on _L_ 2 that is also an algebra homomorphism of _L_ 1 to _L_ ∞.[\[156\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-160)

The [Heisenberg uncertainty principle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heisenberg_uncertainty_principle "Heisenberg uncertainty principle") also contains the number π. The uncertainty principle gives a sharp lower bound on the extent to which it is possible to localize a function both in space and in frequency: with our conventions for the Fourier transform,

(∫−∞∞x2\|f(x)\|2dx)(∫−∞∞ξ2\|f^(ξ)\|2dξ)≥(14π∫−∞∞\|f(x)\|2dx)2.{\\displaystyle \\left(\\int \_{-\\infty }^{\\infty }x^{2}\|f(x)\|^{2}\\,dx\\right)\\left(\\int \_{-\\infty }^{\\infty }\\xi ^{2}\|{\\hat {f}}(\\xi )\|^{2}\\,d\\xi \\right)\\geq \\left({\\frac {1}{4\\pi }}\\int \_{-\\infty }^{\\infty }\|f(x)\|^{2}\\,dx\\right)^{2}.}![{\displaystyle \left(\int _{-\infty }^{\infty }x^{2}|f(x)|^{2}\,dx\right)\left(\int _{-\infty }^{\infty }\xi ^{2}|{\hat {f}}(\xi )|^{2}\,d\xi \right)\geq \left({\frac {1}{4\pi }}\int _{-\infty }^{\infty }|f(x)|^{2}\,dx\right)^{2}.}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/08ed97af513c67ad7eb0f44a51c76696255f16ab)

The physical consequence, about the uncertainty in simultaneous position and momentum observations of a [quantum mechanical](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_mechanical "Quantum mechanical") system, is [discussed below](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#Describing_physical_phenomena). The appearance of π in the formulae of Fourier analysis is ultimately a consequence of the [Stone–von Neumann theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stone%E2%80%93von_Neumann_theorem "Stone–von Neumann theorem"), asserting the uniqueness of the [Schrödinger representation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Schr%C3%B6dinger_representation "Schrödinger representation") of the [Heisenberg group](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heisenberg_group "Heisenberg group").[\[157\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-howe-161)

### Gaussian integrals

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/E%5E%28-x%5E2%29.svg/250px-E%5E%28-x%5E2%29.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:E%5E(-x%5E2).svg) A graph of the [Gaussian function](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gaussian_function "Gaussian function") _ƒ_( _x_) = _e_ − _x_ 2. The coloured region between the function and the _x_-axis has area √_π_.

The fields of [probability](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Probability "Probability") and [statistics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Statistics "Statistics") frequently use the [normal distribution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Normal_distribution "Normal distribution") as a simple model for complex phenomena; for example, scientists generally assume that the observational error in experiments follows a normal distribution.[\[158\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-162) The [Gaussian function](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gaussian_function "Gaussian function"), which is the [probability density function](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Probability_density_function "Probability density function") of the normal distribution with [mean](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mean "Mean") μ and [standard deviation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Standard_deviation "Standard deviation") σ, naturally contains π:[\[159\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-GaussProb-163)

f(x)=1σ2πe−(x−μ)2/(2σ2).{\\displaystyle f(x)={1 \\over \\sigma {\\sqrt {2\\pi }}}\\,e^{-(x-\\mu )^{2}/(2\\sigma ^{2})}.}![{\displaystyle f(x)={1 \over \sigma {\sqrt {2\pi }}}\,e^{-(x-\mu )^{2}/(2\sigma ^{2})}.}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/824d182749fb4097ca7bf06c7435c066756892db)

The factor of 12π{\\displaystyle {\\tfrac {1}{\\sqrt {2\\pi }}}}![{\displaystyle {\tfrac {1}{\sqrt {2\pi }}}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7ef676eb866fd962ce8fe724b168c57499b2d8af) makes the area under the graph of f equal to one, as is required for a probability distribution. This follows from a [change of variables](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Integration_by_substitution "Integration by substitution") in the [Gaussian integral](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gaussian_integral "Gaussian integral"):[\[159\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-GaussProb-163)

∫−∞∞e−u2du=π,{\\displaystyle \\int \_{-\\infty }^{\\infty }e^{-u^{2}}\\,du={\\sqrt {\\pi }},}![{\displaystyle \int _{-\infty }^{\infty }e^{-u^{2}}\,du={\sqrt {\pi }},}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ed51e1d471ba646e304ec261b41d16351948c459)

which says that the area under the basic [bell curve](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bell_curve "Bell curve") in the figure is equal to the square root of π.

The [central limit theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Central_limit_theorem "Central limit theorem") explains the central role of normal distributions, and thus of π, in probability and statistics. This theorem is ultimately connected with the [spectral characterization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#Fourier_transform_and_Heisenberg_uncertainty_principle) of π as the eigenvalue associated with the Heisenberg uncertainty principle, and the fact that equality holds in the uncertainty principle only for the Gaussian function.[\[160\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDymMcKean1972Section_2.7-164) Equivalently, π is the unique constant making the Gaussian normal distribution _e_ − _πx_ 2 equal to its own Fourier transform.[\[161\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-165) Indeed, according to [Howe (1980)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#CITEREFHowe1980), the "whole business" of establishing the fundamental theorems of Fourier analysis reduces to the Gaussian integral.[\[157\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-howe-161)

### Topology

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Order-7_triangular_tiling.svg/250px-Order-7_triangular_tiling.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Order-7_triangular_tiling.svg) [Uniformization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Uniformization_theorem "Uniformization theorem") of the [Klein quartic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Klein_quartic "Klein quartic"), a surface of [genus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Genus_(mathematics) "Genus (mathematics)") three and Euler characteristic −4, as a quotient of the [hyperbolic plane](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Poincar%C3%A9_disk_model "Poincaré disk model") by the [symmetry group](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Symmetry_group "Symmetry group") [PSL(2,7)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/PSL(2,7) "PSL(2,7)") of the [Fano plane](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fano_plane "Fano plane"). The hyperbolic area of a fundamental domain is 8 _π_, by Gauss–Bonnet.

The constant π appears in the [Gauss–Bonnet formula](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gauss%E2%80%93Bonnet_formula "Gauss–Bonnet formula") which relates the [differential geometry of surfaces](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Differential_geometry_of_surfaces "Differential geometry of surfaces") to their [topology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Topology "Topology"). Specifically, if a [compact](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Compact_space "Compact space") surface Σ has [Gauss curvature](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gauss_curvature "Gauss curvature") _K_, then

∫ΣKdA=2πχ(Σ){\\displaystyle \\int \_{\\Sigma }K\\,dA=2\\pi \\chi (\\Sigma )}![{\displaystyle \int _{\Sigma }K\,dA=2\pi \chi (\Sigma )}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/00c846ab9590f095656ecf41398c7d91b9f3a00e)

where _χ_(Σ) is the [Euler characteristic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Euler_characteristic "Euler characteristic"), which is an integer.[\[162\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-166) An example is the surface area of a sphere _S_ of curvature 1 (so that its [radius of curvature](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radius_of_curvature "Radius of curvature"), which coincides with its radius, is also 1.) The Euler characteristic of a sphere can be computed from its [homology groups](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Homology_group "Homology group") and is found to be equal to two. Thus we have

A(S)=∫S1dA=2π⋅2=4π{\\displaystyle A(S)=\\int \_{S}1\\,dA=2\\pi \\cdot 2=4\\pi }![{\displaystyle A(S)=\int _{S}1\,dA=2\pi \cdot 2=4\pi }](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f541185019255fc11e6ef909d681e99b9490cf88)

reproducing the formula for the surface area of a sphere of radius 1.

The constant appears in many other integral formulae in topology, in particular, those involving [characteristic classes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Characteristic_class "Characteristic class") via the [Chern–Weil homomorphism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chern%E2%80%93Weil_homomorphism "Chern–Weil homomorphism").[\[163\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-167)

### Cauchy's integral formula

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Factorial05.jpg/250px-Factorial05.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Factorial05.jpg) Complex analytic functions can be visualized as a collection of streamlines and equipotentials, systems of curves intersecting at right angles. Here illustrated is the complex logarithm of the Gamma function.

One of the key tools in [complex analysis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Complex_analysis "Complex analysis") is [contour integration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Contour_integration "Contour integration") of a function over a positively oriented ( [rectifiable](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rectifiable_curve "Rectifiable curve")) [Jordan curve](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jordan_curve "Jordan curve") γ. A form of [Cauchy's integral formula](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cauchy%27s_integral_formula "Cauchy's integral formula") states that if a point _z_ 0 is interior to γ, then[\[164\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-168)

∮γdzz−z0=2πi.{\\displaystyle \\oint \_{\\gamma }{\\frac {dz}{z-z\_{0}}}=2\\pi i.}![{\displaystyle \oint _{\gamma }{\frac {dz}{z-z_{0}}}=2\pi i.}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/17569b627f1050cac4952724e6e56209e44dc425)

Although the curve γ is not a circle, and hence does not have any obvious connection to the constant π, a standard proof of this result uses [Morera's theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Morera%27s_theorem "Morera's theorem"), which implies that the integral is invariant under [homotopy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Homotopy "Homotopy") of the curve, so that it can be deformed to a circle and then integrated explicitly in polar coordinates. More generally, it is true that if a rectifiable closed curve γ does not contain _z_ 0, then the above integral is 2 _πi_ times the [winding number](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Winding_number "Winding number") of the curve.

The general form of Cauchy's integral formula establishes the relationship between the values of a [complex analytic function](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Complex_analytic_function "Complex analytic function") _f_( _z_) on the Jordan curve γ and the value of _f_( _z_) at any interior point _z_ 0 of γ:[\[165\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-169)

∮γf(z)z−z0dz=2πif(z0){\\displaystyle \\oint \_{\\gamma }{f(z) \\over z-z\_{0}}\\,dz=2\\pi if(z\_{0})}![{\displaystyle \oint _{\gamma }{f(z) \over z-z_{0}}\,dz=2\pi if(z_{0})}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f239d18251cef9208918cba90587fe2d7f618ae2)

provided _f_( _z_) is analytic in the region enclosed by γ and extends continuously to γ. Cauchy's integral formula is a special case of the [residue theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Residue_theorem "Residue theorem"), that if _g_( _z_) is a [meromorphic function](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Meromorphic_function "Meromorphic function") the region enclosed by γ and is continuous in a neighbourhood of γ, then

∮γg(z)dz=2πi∑Res⁡(g,ak){\\displaystyle \\oint \_{\\gamma }g(z)\\,dz=2\\pi i\\sum \\operatorname {Res} (g,a\_{k})}![{\displaystyle \oint _{\gamma }g(z)\,dz=2\pi i\sum \operatorname {Res} (g,a_{k})}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/36fffa5445c67252fba1e631674f6202925ebe3a)

where the sum is of the [residues](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Residue_(mathematics) "Residue (mathematics)") at the [poles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pole_(complex_analysis) "Pole (complex analysis)") of _g_( _z_).

### Vector calculus and physics

The constant π is ubiquitous in [vector calculus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vector_calculus "Vector calculus") and [potential theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Potential_theory "Potential theory"), for example in [Coulomb's law](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coulomb%27s_law "Coulomb's law"),[\[166\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-170) [Gauss's law](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gauss%27s_law "Gauss's law"), [Maxwell's equations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maxwell%27s_equations "Maxwell's equations"), and even the [Einstein field equations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Einstein_field_equations "Einstein field equations").[\[167\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-171)[\[168\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-172) Perhaps the simplest example of this is the two-dimensional [Newtonian potential](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Newtonian_potential "Newtonian potential"), representing the potential of a point source at the origin, whose associated field has unit outward [flux](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Flux "Flux") through any smooth and oriented closed surface enclosing the source:

Φ(x)=12πlog⁡\|x\|.{\\displaystyle \\Phi (\\mathbf {x} )={\\frac {1}{2\\pi }}\\log \|\\mathbf {x} \|.}![{\displaystyle \Phi (\mathbf {x} )={\frac {1}{2\pi }}\log |\mathbf {x} |.}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/173b53b5c1644e0cd4d59433349d3faf3ac98d72)

The factor of 1/2π{\\displaystyle 1/2\\pi }![{\displaystyle 1/2\pi }](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/2d777af7ccaf5b0339d298cedf9757fe87586da1) is necessary to ensure that Φ{\\displaystyle \\Phi }![{\displaystyle \Phi }](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/aed80a2011a3912b028ba32a52dfa57165455f24) is the [fundamental solution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fundamental_solution "Fundamental solution") of the [Poisson equation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Poisson_equation "Poisson equation") in R2{\\displaystyle \\mathbb {R} ^{2}}![{\displaystyle \mathbb {R} ^{2}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e150115ab9f63023215109595b76686a1ff890fd):[\[169\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-Elliptic_PDE2-173)

ΔΦ=δ{\\displaystyle \\Delta \\Phi =\\delta }![{\displaystyle \Delta \Phi =\delta }](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ed7b32c7fab3ef26726f4d1040a0e553d1e99eb9)

where δ{\\displaystyle \\delta }![{\displaystyle \delta }](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c5321cfa797202b3e1f8620663ff43c4660ea03a) is the [Dirac delta function](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dirac_delta_function "Dirac delta function").

In higher dimensions, factors of π are present because of a normalization by the n-dimensional volume of the unit [n sphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/N_sphere "N sphere"). For example, in three dimensions, the Newtonian potential is:[\[169\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-Elliptic_PDE2-173)

Φ(x)=−14π\|x\|,{\\displaystyle \\Phi (\\mathbf {x} )=-{\\frac {1}{4\\pi \|\\mathbf {x} \|}},}![{\displaystyle \Phi (\mathbf {x} )=-{\frac {1}{4\pi |\mathbf {x} |}},}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d621580a0535c73a9a18911166a0c95010d47c3e)

which has the 2-dimensional volume (i.e., the area) of the unit 2-sphere in the denominator.

### Total curvature

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Winding_Number_Around_Point.svg/250px-Winding_Number_Around_Point.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Winding_Number_Around_Point.svg) This curve has total curvature 6 _π_ and turning number 3; it has [winding number](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Winding_number "Winding number") 2 about p and an additional loop which does not contain p.

In the [differential geometry of curves](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Differential_geometry_of_curves "Differential geometry of curves"), the _[total curvature](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Total_curvature "Total curvature")_ of a smooth plane curve is the amount it turns anticlockwise, in radians, from start to finish, computed as the integral of signed [curvature](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Curvature "Curvature") with respect to arc length:

∫abk(s)ds{\\displaystyle \\int \_{a}^{b}k(s)\\,ds}![{\displaystyle \int _{a}^{b}k(s)\,ds}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/931e270d1b5571b7eb17b1e51093efe82f6a12fc)

For a closed curve, this quantity is equal to 2 _πN_ for an integer N called the _[turning number](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Turning_number "Turning number")_ or _index_ of the curve. N is the [winding number](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Winding_number "Winding number") about the origin of the [hodograph](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hodograph "Hodograph") of the curve parametrized by arclength, a new curve lying on the unit circle, described by the normalized [tangent vector](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tangent_vector "Tangent vector") at each point on the original curve. Equivalently, N is the [degree of the map](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Degree_of_a_continuous_mapping "Degree of a continuous mapping") taking each point on the curve to the corresponding point on the hodograph, analogous to the [Gauss map](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gauss_map "Gauss map") for surfaces.

### Gamma function and Stirling's approximation

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Gamma_plot_points_marked.svg/250px-Gamma_plot_points_marked.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Gamma_plot_points_marked.svg) Plot of the gamma function on the real axis

The [factorial](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Factorial "Factorial") function n!{\\displaystyle n!}![{\displaystyle n!}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/bae971720be3cc9b8d82f4cdac89cb89877514a6) is the product of all of the positive integers through n. The [gamma function](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gamma_function "Gamma function") extends the concept of [factorial](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Factorial "Factorial") (normally defined only for non-negative integers) to all complex numbers, except the negative real integers, with the identity Γ(n)=(n−1)!{\\displaystyle \\Gamma (n)=(n-1)!}![{\displaystyle \Gamma (n)=(n-1)!}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ef3f7eebd96f717c5f1fd154b3905af7fbcabf24). When the gamma function is evaluated at half-integers, the result contains π. For example, Γ(12)=π{\\displaystyle \\Gamma {\\bigl (}{\\tfrac {1}{2}}{\\bigr )}={\\sqrt {\\pi }}}![{\displaystyle \Gamma {\bigl (}{\tfrac {1}{2}}{\bigr )}={\sqrt {\pi }}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b6afa161e0e91dc74cc43fe541d377f80b314768) and Γ(52)=34π{\\textstyle \\Gamma {\\bigl (}{\\tfrac {5}{2}}{\\bigr )}={\\tfrac {3}{4}}{\\sqrt {\\pi }}}![{\textstyle \Gamma {\bigl (}{\tfrac {5}{2}}{\bigr )}={\tfrac {3}{4}}{\sqrt {\pi }}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/55339e33eb7e75fde60d015496696602983c6c03).[\[170\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-174)

The gamma function is defined by its [Weierstrass product](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Weierstrass_product "Weierstrass product") development:[\[171\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-175)

Γ(z)=e−γzz∏n=1∞ez/n1+z/n{\\displaystyle \\Gamma (z)={\\frac {e^{-\\gamma z}}{z}}\\prod \_{n=1}^{\\infty }{\\frac {e^{z/n}}{1+z/n}}}![{\displaystyle \Gamma (z)={\frac {e^{-\gamma z}}{z}}\prod _{n=1}^{\infty }{\frac {e^{z/n}}{1+z/n}}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/39107a18b7680b95e8d2af238137b27fe1221168)

where γ is the [Euler–Mascheroni constant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Euler%E2%80%93Mascheroni_constant "Euler–Mascheroni constant"). Evaluated at ⁠z=12{\\displaystyle z={\\tfrac {1}{2}}}![{\displaystyle z={\tfrac {1}{2}}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/8704f74b7e33c3f4a4d23bc1b341f09939a17b70)⁠ and squared, the equation ⁠γ(12))2=π{\\displaystyle \\textstyle \\gamma {\\bigl (}{\\tfrac {1}{2}}{\\bigr )}{\\vphantom {)}}^{2}=\\pi }![{\displaystyle \textstyle \gamma {\bigl (}{\tfrac {1}{2}}{\bigr )}{\vphantom {)}}^{2}=\pi }](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/cc98c6f81829d694374c8bd7fcdfe4e7ea310a15)⁠ reduces to the Wallis product formula. The gamma function is also connected to the [Riemann zeta function](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Riemann_zeta_function "Riemann zeta function") and identities for the [functional determinant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Functional_determinant "Functional determinant"), in which the constant π [plays an important role](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#Number_theory_and_Riemann_zeta_function).

The gamma function is used to calculate the volume _V_ _n_( _r_) of the [_n_-dimensional ball](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/N-ball "N-ball") of radius _r_ in Euclidean _n_-dimensional space, and the surface area _S_ _n_ −1( _r_) of its boundary, the [( _n_ −1)-dimensional sphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/N-sphere "N-sphere"):[\[172\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-176)

Vn(r)=πn/2Γ(n2+1)rn,{\\displaystyle V\_{n}(r)={\\frac {\\pi ^{n/2}}{\\Gamma {\\bigl (}{\\frac {n}{2}}+1{\\bigr )}}}r^{n},}![{\displaystyle V_{n}(r)={\frac {\pi ^{n/2}}{\Gamma {\bigl (}{\frac {n}{2}}+1{\bigr )}}}r^{n},}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/58111b8e9cf73015f83a1437ec3a2a71e191e3de)

Sn−1(r)=nπn/2Γ(n2+1)rn−1.{\\displaystyle S\_{n-1}(r)={\\frac {n\\pi ^{n/2}}{\\Gamma {\\bigl (}{\\tfrac {n}{2}}+1{\\bigr )}}}r^{n-1}.}![{\displaystyle S_{n-1}(r)={\frac {n\pi ^{n/2}}{\Gamma {\bigl (}{\tfrac {n}{2}}+1{\bigr )}}}r^{n-1}.}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/bbbec1f199018ea873885cf27a49cf35f14ae253)

Further, it follows from the [functional equation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Functional_equation "Functional equation") that

2πr=Sn+1(r)Vn(r).{\\displaystyle 2\\pi r={\\frac {S\_{n+1}(r)}{V\_{n}(r)}}.}![{\displaystyle 2\pi r={\frac {S_{n+1}(r)}{V_{n}(r)}}.}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/807a85f4bb84937de20521b0db1fc83295a48532)

The gamma function can be used to create a simple approximation to the factorial function _n_! for large n: n!∼2πn(ne)n{\\textstyle n!\\sim {\\sqrt {2\\pi n}}\\left({\\frac {n}{e}}\\right)^{n}}![{\textstyle n!\sim {\sqrt {2\pi n}}\left({\frac {n}{e}}\right)^{n}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4f32274cb48ebb4eb4b2379884758a61f17c05db) which is known as [Stirling's approximation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stirling%27s_approximation "Stirling's approximation").[\[173\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-177) Equivalently,

π=limn→∞e2nn!22n2n+1.{\\displaystyle \\pi =\\lim \_{n\\to \\infty }{\\frac {e^{2n}n!^{2}}{2n^{2n+1}}}.}![{\displaystyle \pi =\lim _{n\to \infty }{\frac {e^{2n}n!^{2}}{2n^{2n+1}}}.}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/04bedea644b29f119cf4d2175576c12d61010d01)

As a geometrical application of Stirling's approximation, let Δ_n_ denote the [standard simplex](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Simplex "Simplex") in _n_-dimensional Euclidean space, and ( _n_ \+ 1)Δ_n_ denote the simplex having all of its sides scaled up by a factor of _n_ \+ 1. Then

Vol⁡((n+1)Δn)=(n+1)nn!∼en+12πn.{\\displaystyle \\operatorname {Vol} ((n+1)\\Delta \_{n})={\\frac {(n+1)^{n}}{n!}}\\sim {\\frac {e^{n+1}}{\\sqrt {2\\pi n}}}.}![{\displaystyle \operatorname {Vol} ((n+1)\Delta _{n})={\frac {(n+1)^{n}}{n!}}\sim {\frac {e^{n+1}}{\sqrt {2\pi n}}}.}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/fae31c667990be5b0307c3ab5233509497d33bac)

[Ehrhart's volume conjecture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ehrhart%27s_volume_conjecture "Ehrhart's volume conjecture") is that this is the (optimal) upper bound on the volume of a [convex body](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Convex_body "Convex body") containing only one [integer lattice point](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Integer_lattice_point "Integer lattice point").[\[174\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-178)

### Number theory and Riemann zeta function

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Pr%C3%BCfer.png/250px-Pr%C3%BCfer.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Pr%C3%BCfer.png) Each prime has an associated [Prüfer group](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pr%C3%BCfer_group "Prüfer group"), which are arithmetic localizations of the circle. The [L-functions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/L-function "L-function") of analytic number theory are also localized in each prime _p_.[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/ModularGroup-FundamentalDomain.svg/250px-ModularGroup-FundamentalDomain.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:ModularGroup-FundamentalDomain.svg) Solution of the Basel problem using the [Weil conjecture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Weil_conjecture_on_Tamagawa_numbers "Weil conjecture on Tamagawa numbers"): the value of _ζ_(2) is the [hyperbolic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Poincar%C3%A9_half-plane_model "Poincaré half-plane model") area of a fundamental domain of the [modular group](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Modular_group "Modular group"), times _π_/2.

The [Riemann zeta function](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Riemann_zeta_function "Riemann zeta function") _ζ_( _s_) is used in many areas of mathematics. When evaluated at _s_ = 2 it can be written as

ζ(2)=112+122+132+⋯{\\displaystyle \\zeta (2)={\\frac {1}{1^{2}}}+{\\frac {1}{2^{2}}}+{\\frac {1}{3^{2}}}+\\cdots }![{\displaystyle \zeta (2)={\frac {1}{1^{2}}}+{\frac {1}{2^{2}}}+{\frac {1}{3^{2}}}+\cdots }](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/581714c279e80acf87edfb221004f575d4770e97)

Finding a [simple solution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Closed-form_expression "Closed-form expression") for this infinite series was a famous problem in mathematics called the [Basel problem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Basel_problem "Basel problem"). [Leonhard Euler](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Leonhard_Euler "Leonhard Euler") solved it in 1735 when he showed it was equal to _π_ 2/6.[\[83\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPosamentierLehmann2004284-86) Euler's result leads to the [number theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Number_theory "Number theory") result that the probability of two random numbers being [relatively prime](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Relatively_prime "Relatively prime") (that is, having no shared factors) is equal to 6/ _π_ 2.[\[175\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-179) This probability is based on the observation that the probability that any number is [divisible](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Divisible "Divisible") by a prime p is 1/ _p_ (for example, every 7th integer is divisible by 7). Hence the probability that two numbers are both divisible by this prime is 1/ _p_ 2, and the probability that at least one of them is not is 1 − 1/ _p_ 2. For distinct primes, these divisibility events are mutually independent; so the probability that two numbers are relatively prime is given by a product over all primes:[\[176\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-180)

∏p∞(1−1p2)=(∏p∞11−p−2)−1=11+122+132+⋯=1ζ(2)=6π2≈61%.{\\displaystyle {\\begin{aligned}\\prod \_{p}^{\\infty }\\left(1-{\\frac {1}{p^{2}}}\\right)&=\\left(\\prod \_{p}^{\\infty }{\\frac {1}{1-p^{-2}}}\\right)^{-1}\\\\[4pt\]&={\\frac {1}{1+{\\frac {1}{2^{2}}}+{\\frac {1}{3^{2}}}+\\cdots }}\\\\[4pt\]&={\\frac {1}{\\zeta (2)}}={\\frac {6}{\\pi ^{2}}}\\approx 61\\%.\\end{aligned}}}![{\displaystyle {\begin{aligned}\prod _{p}^{\infty }\left(1-{\frac {1}{p^{2}}}\right)&=\left(\prod _{p}^{\infty }{\frac {1}{1-p^{-2}}}\right)^{-1}\\[4pt]&={\frac {1}{1+{\frac {1}{2^{2}}}+{\frac {1}{3^{2}}}+\cdots }}\\[4pt]&={\frac {1}{\zeta (2)}}={\frac {6}{\pi ^{2}}}\approx 61\%.\end{aligned}}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/33ad11b6609d91487577949c7a42872afdc33a36)

This probability can be used in conjunction with a [random number generator](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Random_number_generator "Random number generator") to approximate π using a Monte Carlo approach.[\[177\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-181)

The solution to the Basel problem implies that the geometrically derived quantity π is connected in a deep way to the distribution of prime numbers. This is a special case of [Weil's conjecture on Tamagawa numbers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Weil%27s_conjecture_on_Tamagawa_numbers "Weil's conjecture on Tamagawa numbers"), which asserts the equality of similar such infinite products of _arithmetic_ quantities, localized at each prime _p_, and a _geometrical_ quantity: the reciprocal of the volume of a certain [locally symmetric space](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Locally_symmetric_space "Locally symmetric space"). In the case of the Basel problem, it is the [hyperbolic 3-manifold](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hyperbolic_3-manifold "Hyperbolic 3-manifold") [SL2( **R**)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SL2(R) "SL2(R)")/ [SL2( **Z**)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Modular_group "Modular group").[\[178\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-182)

The zeta function also satisfies Riemann's functional equation, which involves π as well as the gamma function:

ζ(s)=2sπs−1sin⁡(πs2)Γ(1−s)ζ(1−s).{\\displaystyle \\zeta (s)=2^{s}\\pi ^{s-1}\ \\sin \\left({\\frac {\\pi s}{2}}\\right)\ \\Gamma (1-s)\ \\zeta (1-s).}![{\displaystyle \zeta (s)=2^{s}\pi ^{s-1}\ \sin \left({\frac {\pi s}{2}}\right)\ \Gamma (1-s)\ \zeta (1-s).}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/37b4eab44e38b188e9cdde9f8d8344ca79a980ec)

Furthermore, the derivative of the zeta function satisfies

exp⁡(−ζ′(0))=2π.{\\displaystyle \\exp(-\\zeta '(0))={\\sqrt {2\\pi }}.}![{\displaystyle \exp(-\zeta '(0))={\sqrt {2\pi }}.}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d2907a3d1a8927b4fd2a276f0463569cc1f77b6f)

A consequence is that π can be obtained from the [functional determinant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Functional_determinant "Functional determinant") of the [harmonic oscillator](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Harmonic_oscillator "Harmonic oscillator"). This functional determinant can be computed via a product expansion, and is equivalent to the Wallis product formula.[\[179\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-183) The calculation can be recast in [quantum mechanics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_mechanics "Quantum mechanics"), specifically the [variational approach](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Calculus_of_variations "Calculus of variations") to the [spectrum of the hydrogen atom](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bohr_model "Bohr model").[\[180\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-184)

### Fourier series

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/2-adic_integers_with_dual_colorings.svg/250px-2-adic_integers_with_dual_colorings.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:2-adic_integers_with_dual_colorings.svg) π appears in characters of [p-adic numbers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/P-adic_numbers "P-adic numbers") (shown), which are elements of a [Prüfer group](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pr%C3%BCfer_group "Prüfer group"). [Tate's thesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tate%27s_thesis "Tate's thesis") makes heavy use of this machinery.[\[181\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-185)

The constant π also appears naturally in [Fourier series](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fourier_series "Fourier series") of [periodic functions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Periodic_function "Periodic function"). Periodic functions are functions on the group **T** = **R**/ **Z** of fractional parts of real numbers. The Fourier decomposition shows that a complex-valued function f on **T** can be written as an infinite linear superposition of [unitary characters](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Unitary_character "Unitary character") of **T**. That is, continuous [group homomorphisms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Group_homomorphism "Group homomorphism") from **T** to the [circle group](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Circle_group "Circle group") _U_(1) of unit modulus complex numbers. It is a theorem that every character of **T** is one of the complex exponentials en(x)=e2πinx{\\displaystyle e\_{n}(x)=e^{2\\pi inx}}![{\displaystyle e_{n}(x)=e^{2\pi inx}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/142bcbcd2610f5640cdfd50166a105624164d007).

It is a theorem that every character of **T** is one of the complex exponentials en(x)=e2πinx{\\displaystyle e\_{n}(x)=e^{2\\pi inx}}![{\displaystyle e_{n}(x)=e^{2\pi inx}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/142bcbcd2610f5640cdfd50166a105624164d007). Among these, there is a unique character, up to complex conjugation, that is a group isomorphism from **T** onto the multiplicative group of complex numbers of absolute value one.[\[182\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-186) A definition of the constant, due to [Nicolas Bourbaki](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nicolas_Bourbaki "Nicolas Bourbaki"), takes π to be half the magnitude of the derivative of this isomorphism.[\[183\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-Nicolas_Bourbaki-187)

Equivalently, using the [Haar measure](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Haar_measure "Haar measure") on the circle group, the other characters have derivatives whose magnitudes are positive integral multiples of 2π. Thus π is the unique positive number such that the group **T**, equipped with its Haar measure, is [Pontrjagin dual](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pontrjagin_dual "Pontrjagin dual") to the [lattice](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lattice_(group) "Lattice (group)") of integral multiples of 2π.[\[183\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-Nicolas_Bourbaki-187)[\[184\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDymMcKean1972Chapter_4-188) This is a version of the one-dimensional [Poisson summation formula](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Poisson_summation_formula "Poisson summation formula").

In Fourier analysis, the appearance of π rather than 2π often signals the passage from ordinary characters to [projective representations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Projective_representation "Projective representation"). The basic exponential eπix{\\displaystyle e^{\\pi ix}}![{\displaystyle e^{\pi ix}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/541c6892dbf16345cb4bf7bb8b066b81f2ffe7de) is not a character of the group **T**, since it changes sign after one turn of the circle; instead, it defines a representation of the [double cover](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Double_covering_group "Double covering group") of **T**. This is the simplest example of a projective representation, and the same phenomenon recurs more broadly in harmonic analysis. [Spinors](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spinor "Spinor"), for example, represent rotations only after passing to a double cover, and [metaplectic representations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metaplectic_representation "Metaplectic representation") similarly arise from a double cover of [SL(2, **R**)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SL(2,R) "SL(2,R)"). The same ideas also connect Fourier analysis with arithmetic, since theta functions and modular forms of half-integral weight are naturally governed by these metaplectic and Heisenberg-theoretic constructions.

### Modular forms and theta functions

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/ca/Lattice_with_tau.svg/250px-Lattice_with_tau.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Lattice_with_tau.svg) Theta functions transform under the [lattice](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lattice_(group) "Lattice (group)") of periods of an elliptic curve.

The constant π is connected in a deep way with the theory of [modular forms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Modular_form "Modular form") and [theta functions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Theta_function "Theta function"). For example, the [Chudnovsky algorithm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chudnovsky_algorithm "Chudnovsky algorithm") involves in an essential way the [j-invariant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/J-invariant "J-invariant") of an [elliptic curve](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Elliptic_curve "Elliptic curve").

[Modular forms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Modular_form "Modular form") are [holomorphic functions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Holomorphic_function "Holomorphic function") in the [upper half plane](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Upper_half_plane "Upper half plane") characterized by their transformation properties under the [modular group](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Modular_group "Modular group")SL2(Z){\\displaystyle \\mathrm {SL} \_{2}(\\mathbb {Z} )}![{\displaystyle \mathrm {SL} _{2}(\mathbb {Z} )}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ca2d95ba975aa85bd6c46f0a696872d3aa385869) (or its various subgroups), a lattice in the group ⁠SL2(R){\\displaystyle \\mathrm {SL} \_{2}(\\mathbb {R} )}![{\displaystyle \mathrm {SL} _{2}(\mathbb {R} )}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/0bb95adfe7c34f38de771bb5fa44e17ec26deeee)⁠. An example is the [Jacobi theta function](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jacobi_theta_function "Jacobi theta function")

θ(z,τ)=∑n=−∞∞e2πinz+πin2τ{\\displaystyle \\theta (z,\\tau )=\\sum \_{n=-\\infty }^{\\infty }e^{2\\pi inz\ +\ \\pi in^{2}\\tau }}![{\displaystyle \theta (z,\tau )=\sum _{n=-\infty }^{\infty }e^{2\pi inz\ +\ \pi in^{2}\tau }}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e1f50d5773dc1a50d50774df58ca32b47d507f26)

which is a kind of modular form called a [Jacobi form](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jacobi_form "Jacobi form").[\[185\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-Mumford_1983_1%E2%80%93117-189) This is sometimes written in terms of the [nome](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nome_(mathematics) "Nome (mathematics)")q=eπiτ{\\displaystyle q=e^{\\pi i\\tau }}![{\displaystyle q=e^{\pi i\tau }}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b04b5fc2797318028727bd06733bb52300c99e9b).

The constant π is the unique constant making the Jacobi theta function an [automorphic form](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Automorphic_form "Automorphic form"), which means that it transforms in a specific way. Certain identities hold for all automorphic forms. An example is

θ(z+τ,τ)=e−πiτ−2πizθ(z,τ),{\\displaystyle \\theta (z+\\tau ,\\tau )=e^{-\\pi i\\tau -2\\pi iz}\\theta (z,\\tau ),}![{\displaystyle \theta (z+\tau ,\tau )=e^{-\pi i\tau -2\pi iz}\theta (z,\tau ),}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/94df3eda20c1a72c699a40cf8b4e0ef49801f601)

which implies that θ transforms as a representation under the discrete [Heisenberg group](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heisenberg_group "Heisenberg group"). General modular forms and other [theta functions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Theta_function "Theta function") also involve π, once again because of the [Stone–von Neumann theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stone%E2%80%93von_Neumann_theorem "Stone–von Neumann theorem").[\[185\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-Mumford_1983_1%E2%80%93117-189)

### Cauchy distribution and potential theory

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Witch_of_Agnesi%2C_construction.svg/250px-Witch_of_Agnesi%2C_construction.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Witch_of_Agnesi,_construction.svg) The [Witch of Agnesi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Witch_of_Agnesi "Witch of Agnesi"), named for [Maria Agnesi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maria_Gaetana_Agnesi "Maria Gaetana Agnesi") (1718–1799), is a geometrical construction of the graph of the Cauchy distribution.[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/2d_random_walk_ag_adatom_ag111.gif/250px-2d_random_walk_ag_adatom_ag111.gif)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:2d_random_walk_ag_adatom_ag111.gif) The Cauchy distribution governs the passage of [Brownian particles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brownian_motion "Brownian motion") through a membrane.

The [Cauchy distribution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cauchy_distribution "Cauchy distribution")

g(x)=1π⋅1x2+1{\\displaystyle g(x)={\\frac {1}{\\pi }}\\cdot {\\frac {1}{x^{2}+1}}}![{\displaystyle g(x)={\frac {1}{\pi }}\cdot {\frac {1}{x^{2}+1}}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/19fbbd1733585d1552298a9352029f90887c23a8)

is a [probability density function](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Probability_density_function "Probability density function"). The total probability is equal to one, owing to the integral:

∫−∞∞1x2+1dx=π.{\\displaystyle \\int \_{-\\infty }^{\\infty }{\\frac {1}{x^{2}+1}}\\,dx=\\pi .}![{\displaystyle \int _{-\infty }^{\infty }{\frac {1}{x^{2}+1}}\,dx=\pi .}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c1f17dafb3b274d126111151564a8666bb4ac93a)

The [Shannon entropy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shannon_entropy "Shannon entropy") of the Cauchy distribution is equal to ln(4 _π_), which also involves π.

The Cauchy distribution plays an important role in [potential theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Potential_theory "Potential theory") because it is the simplest [Furstenberg measure](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Furstenberg_boundary "Furstenberg boundary"), the classical [Poisson kernel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Poisson_kernel "Poisson kernel") associated with a [Brownian motion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brownian_motion "Brownian motion") in a half-plane.[\[186\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-190) [Conjugate harmonic functions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conjugate_harmonic_function "Conjugate harmonic function") and so also the [Hilbert transform](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hilbert_transform "Hilbert transform") are associated with the asymptotics of the Poisson kernel. The Hilbert transform _H_ is the integral transform given by the [Cauchy principal value](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cauchy_principal_value "Cauchy principal value") of the [singular integral](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Singular_integral "Singular integral")

Hf(t)=1π∫−∞∞f(x)dxx−t.{\\displaystyle Hf(t)={\\frac {1}{\\pi }}\\int \_{-\\infty }^{\\infty }{\\frac {f(x)\\,dx}{x-t}}.}![{\displaystyle Hf(t)={\frac {1}{\pi }}\int _{-\infty }^{\infty }{\frac {f(x)\,dx}{x-t}}.}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/2cd815ba69c72a4cb8e65180491f6db68eda5a12)

The constant π is the unique (positive) normalizing factor such that _H_ defines a [linear complex structure](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Linear_complex_structure "Linear complex structure") on the [Hilbert space](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hilbert_space "Hilbert space") of square-integrable real-valued functions on the real line.[\[187\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-191) The Hilbert transform, like the Fourier transform, can be characterized purely in terms of its transformation properties on the Hilbert space L2( **R**): up to a normalization factor, it is the unique bounded linear operator that commutes with positive dilations and anti-commutes with all reflections of the real line.[\[188\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-192) The constant π is the unique normalizing factor that makes this transformation unitary.

### In the Mandelbrot set

[![An complex black shape on a blue background.](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Mandel_zoom_00_mandelbrot_set.jpg/250px-Mandel_zoom_00_mandelbrot_set.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Mandel_zoom_00_mandelbrot_set.jpg) The [Mandelbrot set](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mandelbrot_set "Mandelbrot set") can be used to approximate π.

An occurrence of π in the [fractal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fractal "Fractal") called the [Mandelbrot set](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mandelbrot_set "Mandelbrot set") was discovered by David Boll in 1991. He examined the behaviour of the Mandelbrot set near the "neck" at (−0.75, 0). When the number of iterations until divergence for the point (−0.75, _ε_) is multiplied by ε, the result approaches π as ε approaches zero. The point (0.25 + _ε_, 0) at the cusp of the large "valley" on the right side of the Mandelbrot set behaves similarly: the number of iterations until divergence multiplied by the square root of ε tends to π.[\[189\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-193)

## Outside mathematics

### Describing physical phenomena

Although not a [physical constant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Physical_constant "Physical constant"), π appears routinely in equations describing physical phenomena, often because of π's relationship to the circle and to [spherical coordinate systems](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spherical_coordinate_system "Spherical coordinate system"). A simple formula from the field of [classical mechanics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Classical_mechanics "Classical mechanics") gives the approximate period T of a simple [pendulum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pendulum "Pendulum") of length L, swinging with a small amplitude (g is the [earth's gravitational acceleration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gravity_of_Earth "Gravity of Earth")):[\[190\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-194)

T≈2πLg.{\\displaystyle T\\approx 2\\pi {\\sqrt {\\frac {L}{g}}}.}![{\displaystyle T\approx 2\pi {\sqrt {\frac {L}{g}}}.}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/bd035c36f4530cfdbe4d650dad9a9fb96600c271)

One of the key formulae of [quantum mechanics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_mechanics "Quantum mechanics") is [Heisenberg's uncertainty principle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heisenberg%27s_uncertainty_principle "Heisenberg's uncertainty principle"), which shows that the uncertainty in the measurement of a [particle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Subatomic_particle "Subatomic particle")'s position (Δ _x_) and [momentum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Momentum "Momentum") (Δ _p_) cannot both be arbitrarily small at the same time (where h is the [Planck constant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planck_constant "Planck constant")):[\[191\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-195)

ΔxΔp≥h4π.{\\displaystyle \\Delta x\\,\\Delta p\\geq {\\frac {h}{4\\pi }}.}![{\displaystyle \Delta x\,\Delta p\geq {\frac {h}{4\pi }}.}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f2897ef997b77cd45b82bf7f217d4a614fefdbfa)

The fact that π is approximately equal to 3 plays a role in the relatively long lifetime of [orthopositronium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orthopositronium "Orthopositronium"). The inverse lifetime to lowest order in the [fine-structure constant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fine-structure_constant "Fine-structure constant") α is[\[192\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-196)

1τ=2π2−99πmeα6,{\\displaystyle {\\frac {1}{\\tau }}=2{\\frac {\\pi ^{2}-9}{9\\pi }}m\_{\\text{e}}\\alpha ^{6},}![{\displaystyle {\frac {1}{\tau }}=2{\frac {\pi ^{2}-9}{9\pi }}m_{\text{e}}\alpha ^{6},}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/0d51fbd59e6edefd2ddcb3f4843642da06d32de5)

where _m_ e is the mass of the [electron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electron "Electron").

π is present in some structural engineering formulae, such as the [buckling](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Buckling "Buckling") formula derived by Euler, which gives the maximum axial load F that a long, slender column of length L, [modulus of elasticity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Modulus_of_elasticity "Modulus of elasticity") E, and [area moment of inertia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Area_moment_of_inertia "Area moment of inertia") I can carry without buckling:[\[193\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-197)

F=π2EIL2.{\\displaystyle F={\\frac {\\pi ^{2}EI}{L^{2}}}.}![{\displaystyle F={\frac {\pi ^{2}EI}{L^{2}}}.}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/94ec2b531f168b03efdfcdfd27061df942e2a4c4)

The field of [fluid dynamics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fluid_dynamics "Fluid dynamics") contains π in [Stokes' law](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stokes%27_law "Stokes' law"), which approximates the [frictional force](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Drag_force "Drag force") F exerted on small, [spherical](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sphere "Sphere") objects of radius R, moving with velocity v in a [fluid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fluid "Fluid") with [dynamic viscosity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dynamic_viscosity "Dynamic viscosity") η:[\[194\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-198)

F=6πηRv.{\\displaystyle F=6\\pi \\eta Rv.}![{\displaystyle F=6\pi \eta Rv.}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9e327b1b8e506d7dd945915675ce1ad287e53cdc)

In electromagnetics, the [vacuum permeability](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vacuum_permeability "Vacuum permeability") constant _μ_ 0 appears in [Maxwell's equations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maxwell%27s_equations "Maxwell's equations"), which describe the properties of [electric](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electric_field "Electric field") and [magnetic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnetic_field "Magnetic field") fields and [electromagnetic radiation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation "Electromagnetic radiation"). Before 20 May 2019, it was defined as exactly

μ0=4π×10−7 H/m≈1.2566370614…×10−6 N/A2.{\\displaystyle \\mu \_{0}=4\\pi \\times 10^{-7}{\\text{ H/m}}\\approx 1.2566370614\\ldots \\times 10^{-6}{\\text{ N/A}}^{2}.}![{\displaystyle \mu _{0}=4\pi \times 10^{-7}{\text{ H/m}}\approx 1.2566370614\ldots \times 10^{-6}{\text{ N/A}}^{2}.}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e8fa061c51aebf2f41aee0bbc787b6f599fd4019)

### Memorizing digits

Main article: [Piphilology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Piphilology "Piphilology")

[Piphilology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Piphilology "Piphilology") is the practice of memorizing large numbers of digits of π,[\[195\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel200644%E2%80%9345-199) and world records are kept by _[Guinness World Records](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Guinness_World_Records "Guinness World Records")_.
