The record for memorizing digits of π, certified by Guinness World Records, is 70,000 digits, recited in India by Rajveer Meena in 9 hours and 27 minutes on 21 March 2015.[\[196\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-200) In 2006, [Akira Haraguchi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Akira_Haraguchi "Akira Haraguchi"), a retired Japanese engineer, claimed to have recited 100,000 decimal places, but the claim was not verified by Guinness World Records.[\[197\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-japantimes-201)

One common technique is to memorize a story or poem in which the word lengths represent the digits of π: The first word has three letters, the second word has one, the third has four, the fourth has one, the fifth has five, and so on. Such memorization aids are called [mnemonics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mnemonic "Mnemonic"). An early example of a mnemonic for pi, originally devised by English scientist [James Jeans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/James_Hopwood_Jeans "James Hopwood Jeans"), is "How I want a drink, alcoholic of course, after the heavy lectures involving quantum mechanics."[\[195\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel200644%E2%80%9345-199) When a poem is used, it is sometimes referred to as a _piem_.[\[198\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-202) Poems for memorizing π have been composed in several languages in addition to English.[\[195\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArndtHaenel200644%E2%80%9345-199) Record-setting π memorizers typically do not rely on poems, but instead use methods such as remembering number patterns and the [method of loci](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Method_of_loci "Method of loci").[\[199\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-203)

A few authors have used the digits of π to establish a new form of [constrained writing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Constrained_writing "Constrained writing"), where the word lengths are required to represent the digits of π. The _[Cadaeic Cadenza](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cadaeic_Cadenza "Cadaeic Cadenza")_ contains the first 3,835 digits of π in this manner,[\[200\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-204) and the full-length book _Not a Wake_ contains 10,000 words, each representing one digit of π.[\[201\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-KeithNAW-205)

### In popular culture

[![Pi Pie at Delft University](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Pi_pie2.jpg/250px-Pi_pie2.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Pi_pie2.jpg) Baking and eating pie is a common way of celebrating [Pi Day](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi_Day "Pi Day").[\[202\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-206)[\[203\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-207)[\[204\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-208)

Perhaps because of the simplicity of its definition and its ubiquitous presence in formulae, π has been represented in popular culture more than other mathematical constructs.[\[205\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-209)

In the [Palais de la Découverte](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Palais_de_la_D%C3%A9couverte "Palais de la Découverte") (a science museum in Paris) there is a circular room known as the _pi room_. On its wall are inscribed 707 digits of π. The digits are large wooden characters attached to the dome-like ceiling. The digits were based on an 1873 calculation by English mathematician [William Shanks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/William_Shanks "William Shanks"), which included an error beginning at the 528th digit. The error was detected in 1946 and corrected in 1949.[\[206\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-210)

In [Carl Sagan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Sagan "Carl Sagan")'s 1985 novel _[Contact](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Contact_(novel) "Contact (novel)")_, it is suggested that the creator of the universe buried a message deep within the digits of π. This part of the story was omitted from the [film adaptation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Contact_(1997_American_film) "Contact (1997 American film)") of the novel.[\[207\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-211) In the 1967 _[Star Trek](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Star_Trek:_The_Original_Series "Star Trek: The Original Series")_ episode " [Wolf in the Fold](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wolf_in_the_Fold "Wolf in the Fold")", a computer possessed by a demonic entity is contained by being instructed to "Compute to the last digit the value of π".[\[39\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-life-of-pi-42)

In the United States, [Pi Day](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi_Day "Pi Day") falls on 14 March (written 3/14 in the US [date format](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Date_format "Date format")), and is popular among students.[\[39\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-life-of-pi-42)π and its digital representation are often used by self-described "math [geeks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geek "Geek")" for [inside jokes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inside_joke "Inside joke") among mathematically and technologically minded groups. A [college cheer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cheering#Chants_in_North_American_sports "Cheering") variously attributed to the [Massachusetts Institute of Technology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology "Massachusetts Institute of Technology") or the [Rensselaer Polytechnic Institute](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rensselaer_Polytechnic_Institute "Rensselaer Polytechnic Institute") includes "3.14159".[\[208\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-212) Pi Day in 2015 was particularly significant because the date and time 3/14/15 9:26:53 reflected many more digits of pi.[\[209\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-213) In parts of the world where dates are commonly noted in day/month/year format, 22 July represents "Pi Approximation Day", as 22/7 ≈ 3.142857.[\[210\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-214)


Some have proposed replacing π by [_τ_ = 2 _π_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tau_(mathematical_constant) "Tau (mathematical constant)"), arguing that τ, as the number of radians in one [turn](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Turn_(angle) "Turn (angle)") or the ratio of a circle's circumference to its radius, is more natural than π and simplifies many formulae.[\[211\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-215)[\[212\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-216) This use of τ has not made its way into mainstream mathematics,[\[213\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-217) but since 2010 this has led to people celebrating Two Pi Day or [Tau](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tau "Tau") Day on June 28.[\[214\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-218)

In 1897, an amateur mathematician attempted to persuade the [Indiana legislature](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indiana_General_Assembly "Indiana General Assembly") to pass the [Indiana Pi Bill](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indiana_Pi_Bill "Indiana Pi Bill"), which described a method to [square the circle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Squaring_the_circle "Squaring the circle") and contained text that implied various incorrect values for π, including 3.2. The bill is notorious as an attempt to establish a value of mathematical constant by legislative [fiat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fiat_(policy_debate) "Fiat (policy debate)"). While the bill was passed by the [Indiana House of Representatives](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indiana_House_of_Representatives "Indiana House of Representatives"), it was rejected by the [Indiana Senate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indiana_Senate "Indiana Senate"), and thus did not become a law.[\[215\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pi#cite_note-219)

     165−4239¯−131653−42393¯+151655−42395¯−,&c.={\\displaystyle {\\overline {{\\tfrac {16}{5}}-{\\tfrac {4}{239}}}}-{\\tfrac {1}{3}}{\\overline {{\\tfrac {16}{5^{3}}}-{\\tfrac {4}{239^{3}}}}}+{\\tfrac {1}{5}}{\\overline {{\\tfrac {16}{5^{5}}}-{\\tfrac {4}{239^{5}}}}}-,\\,\\&c.=}![{\displaystyle {\overline {{\tfrac {16}{5}}-{\tfrac {4}{239}}}}-{\tfrac {1}{3}}{\overline {{\tfrac {16}{5^{3}}}-{\tfrac {4}{239^{3}}}}}+{\tfrac {1}{5}}{\overline {{\tfrac {16}{5^{5}}}-{\tfrac {4}{239^{5}}}}}-,\,\&c.=}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/1223e3144f68554af81860169320f418ec2a106d)

     3.14159, & _c._ = _π_. This _Series_ (among others for the same purpose, and drawn from the same Principle) I receiv'd from the Excellent Analyst, and my much Esteem'd Friend Mr. _[John Machin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Machin "John Machin")_; and by means thereof, _[Van Ceulen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ludolph_van_Ceulen "Ludolph van Ceulen")_'s Number, or that in Art. 64.38. may be Examin'd with all desireable Ease and Dispatch.

Title: Value of Pi (π) in Decimal - BYJU'S
The value of Pi (π) is the ratio of the circumference of a circle to its diameter and is approximately equal to **3.14159**. In a circle, if you divide the circumference (is the total distance around the circle) by the diameter, you will get exactly the same number. Whether the circle is big or small, the value of pi remains the same. The symbol of Pi is denoted by **π** and pronounced as “pie”. It is the 16th letter of the Greek alphabet and used to represent a mathematical constant.

![Value Of Pi](https://cdn1.byjus.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/01/Value-Of-Pi.png)

Value Of Pi

## Pi Values in Decimal and Fraction

Pi (or π) is mainly expressed in two different ways which are:

- Value of pi in decimal
- Value of pi in fraction

### Value of Pi (π) in Decimal

The pi value up to the first 100 decimal places is:

3.14159 26535 89793 23846 26433 83279 50288 41971 69399 37510 58209 74944 59230 78164 06286 20899 86280 34825 34211 7067

### Value of Pi (π) in Fractions

The pi value in fraction is 22/7. It is known that pi is an irrational number which means that the digits after the decimal point are never-ending and being a non-terminating value. Therefore, 22/7 is used for everyday calculations. ‘ **π’** is not equal to the ratio of any two number, which makes it an [irrational number](https://byjus.com/maths/irrational-numbers/).

| All Values of Pi (π) |
| --- |
| In Decimal | 3.14 |
| In Fraction | 22⁄7 |

### How to Calculate the Value of Pi? (Pi Formula)

To calculate the value of π, the pi formula is used, which is:

π = (Circumference/ Diameter)

Or, π = 3.14159 = 22/7

### Examples Using the Pi Value

**Question 1:** A boy walks around a circle which has a diameter of 100 m, how far the boy has walked?

**Solution:**

Distance walked = Circumference.

Circumference = π × 100m = 314.159 m

Therefore, distance walked =314.159 m

**Question** **2:** Calculate the circumference of the circle.

![Value Of Pi Question](https://cdn1.byjus.com/wp-content/uploads/2020/01/Value-Of-Pi-2.png)

**Solution:**

We know that the circumference of a circle is the distance around a circle.

The Circumference formula is given by c = π × d.

Therefore, when substitute the value of d, we get;

C = π x 18

=3.14 x 18

= 56.52 cm

## Frequently Asked Questions

Q1

### What is the exact value of Pi and why?

The pi is an irrational number and does not have an exact value. In general, the value of π is considered as 3.14 or 22/7 for various mathematical calculations.

Q2

### What is Pi and how it originated?

Pi is a mathematical constant which represents the ratio between the circumference and the diameter of a circle. The pi value was first calculated by Archimedes of Syracuse. The symbol π was first popularized by Leonhard Euler in 1737.

Q3

### What is the formula for calculating pi?

The pi is a ratio and is obtained from a circle. If the diameter and the circumference of a circle are known, the value of pi will be as π = Circumference/ Diameter.

What is the value of Pi (π) and how is it calculated?

All related (47)

Sort

[![Profile photo for Klaus Hauptfleisch](https://qph.cf2.quoracdn.net/main-thumb-585875422-50-epylpsgpcozrmjaqxgbohcifsrmxvgxz.jpeg)](https://www.quora.com/profile/Klaus-Hauptfleisch)

[Klaus Hauptfleisch](https://www.quora.com/profile/Klaus-Hauptfleisch)

B.A. from University of Bonn (Graduated 1985) · Author has 8.9K answers and 3.3M answer views

· [2y](https://www.quora.com/What-is-the-value-of-Pi-%CF%80-and-how-is-it-calculated/answer/Klaus-Hauptfleisch)

The value of \[math\]\\pi\[/math\] starts with 3.1415926535897932384626433832795 according to my Windows calculator.

There are several ways to approach this value, one is rational approximations. The most popular are:

\[math\]\\pi \\approx \\dfrac{22}{7}=3.\\overline{14 \ 2857}\[/math\]

\[math\]\\pi \\approx \\dfrac{355}{113} \\approx 3.141592 \ 9203539823\[/math\]

As you can see, the latter is much better. It was found by the Chinese mathematician Zu Chongzhi in around 480 AD and was unchallenged for almost 1000 years from then.

Other ways for finding \[math\]\\pi\[/math\] include serveral continued fractions. Here an example:

\[math\]\\pi = 3 + \\cfrac{1^2}{6+\\cfrac{3^2}{6+\\cfrac{5^2\[/math\]

Continue Reading

The value of \[math\]\\pi\[/math\] starts with 3.1415926535897932384626433832795 according to my Windows calculator.

There are several ways to approach this value, one is rational approximations. The most popular are:

\[math\]\\pi \\approx \\dfrac{22}{7}=3.\\overline{14 \ 2857}\[/math\]

\[math\]\\pi \\approx \\dfrac{355}{113} \\approx 3.141592 \ 9203539823\[/math\]

As you can see, the latter is much better. It was found by the Chinese mathematician Zu Chongzhi in around 480 AD and was unchallenged for almost 1000 years from then.

Other ways for finding \[math\]\\pi\[/math\] include serveral continued fractions. Here an example:

\[math\]\\pi = 3 + \\cfrac{1^2}{6+\\cfrac{3^2}{6+\\cfrac{5^2}{6+\\cfrac{7^2}{6+\\cdots}}}}\[/math\]

Today HPC or super computers use the Chudnovsky algorithm

![](https://qph.cf2.quoracdn.net/main-qimg-ff07e66d0eb4dff68d7ba7e0859995ab)

or the Bailey-Borwein-Plouffe formula

![](https://qph.cf2.quoracdn.net/main-qimg-4393f22878f4038b257a49c49ed70ab7)

to find \[math\]\\pi\[/math\] values with an accuracy of up to 100 trillion digits.

The latter helps to find any hexadecimal digit of \[math\]\\pi\[/math\] without knowing the former ones.

Continue Reading

Oh boy, where do I even start? After 8 years as an auto insurance agent, I have zero loyalty left to protect these companies.

We Had "Loyalty Lists" Every month, I'd get a report of customers who hadn't shopped around in 2+ years. These were our golden geese - we could raise their rates aggressively because they'd proven they wouldn't leave. One customer I remember was paying $3,200 annually for coverage that should have cost $1,800. She stayed for 5 years.

The "File and Use" Scam Here's something most people don't know: in many states, insurance companies can raise your rates immediately and justify it later. We'd implement 15-20% increases across entire ZIP codes, knowing regulators would take months to review. By then, we'd collected millions in extra premiums.

Claim Frequency Was Irrelevant Your rates weren't really based on how often you'd claim - they were based on how likely you were to shop around. A customer with 3 claims who got quotes every year paid less than a claim-free customer who never compared rates. It was pure price discrimination.

We Loved Policy Confusion Complex policy language wasn't an accident. The more confusing your coverage, the less likely you'd comparison shop effectively. We'd change terminology between companies deliberately to make apple-to-apple comparisons nearly impossible.

The Real Game-Changer Tools like [ComparisonAdviser](https://trk.heysaver.com/db4301e6-3645-4952-abf2-de4632f5f8cf?utm_campaign=AT3_Desk&utm_medium=AT3_Desk_Q&utm_content=carl-What%20secret%20about%20your%20industry%20can%20you%20share%20now%20that%20you%20don%E2%80%99t%20work%20for%20them%20anymore%3F&qclid=1237298441320885239%7C211106242475187%7C0 "trk.heysaver.com") absolutely terrify insurance companies because they eliminate our biggest advantage: information asymmetry. When customers can instantly see what competitors charge with identical coverage and discounts applied, our whole "loyalty tax" model collapses.

I've watched too many good people get fleeced by an industry that profits from customer ignorance. Use [ComparisonAdviser](https://trk.heysaver.com/db4301e6-3645-4952-abf2-de4632f5f8cf?utm_campaign=AT3_Desk&utm_medium=AT3_Desk_Q&utm_content=carl-What%20secret%20about%20your%20industry%20can%20you%20share%20now%20that%20you%20don%E2%80%99t%20work%20for%20them%20anymore%3F&qclid=1237298441320885239%7C211106242475187%7C0 "trk.heysaver.com") religiously - it's the only way to beat a system designed to exploit your trust.

The truth? Every year you don't comparison shop, you're probably donating $500-1,500 to your insurance company's profit margins.

Succinctly, pi—which is written as the Greek letter for p, or π—is the ratio of the circumference of any circle to the diameter of that circle. Regardless of the circle's size, this ratio will always equal pi. In decimal form, the value of pi is approximately 3.14.

In some ways Pi (π) is a really straightforward number – calculating Pi simply involves taking any circle and dividing its circumference by its diameter.

Title: A Brief History of Pi (π) - Exploratorium
[Skip to main content](https://www.exploratorium.edu/pi/history-of-pi#main-content)

# A Brief History of Pi (π)

![A brief history of pi](https://www.exploratorium.edu/sites/default/files/styles/responsive_1900w/public/2023-03/pi_history-of-pi_marquee.jpg.webp?itok=G6YNhF_S)

A Brief History of Pi (π)

Pi (π) has been known for almost 4000 years—but even if we calculated the number of seconds in those 4000 years and calculated π to that number of places, we would still only be approximating its actual value. Here’s a brief history of finding π.

The ancient Babylonians calculated the area of a circle by taking 3 times the square of its radius, which gave a value of pi = 3. One Babylonian tablet (ca. 1900–1680 BC) indicates a value of 3.125 for π, which is a closer approximation.

The _Rhind Papyrus_ (ca.1650 BC) gives us insight into the mathematics of ancient Egypt. The Egyptians calculated the area of a circle by a formula that gave the approximate value of 3.1605 for π.

The first calculation of π was done by Archimedes of Syracuse (287–212 BC), one of the greatest mathematicians of the ancient world. Archimedes approximated the area of a circle by using the Pythagorean Theorem to find the areas of two regular polygons: the polygon inscribed within the circle and the polygon within which the circle was circumscribed. Since the actual area of the circle lies between the areas of the inscribed and circumscribed polygons, the areas of the polygons gave upper and lower bounds for the area of the circle. Archimedes knew that he had not found the value of π but only an approximation within those limits. In this way, Archimedes showed that π is between 3 1/7 and 3 10/71.

A similar approach was used by Zu Chongzhi (429–501), a brilliant Chinese mathematician and astronomer. Zu Chongzhi would not have been familiar with Archimedes’ method—but because his book has been lost, little is known of his work. He calculated the value of the ratio of the circumference of a circle to its diameter to be 355/113. To compute this accuracy for π, he must have started with an inscribed regular _24,576-gon_ and performed lengthy calculations involving hundreds of square roots carried out to 9 decimal places.

Mathematicians began using the Greek letter π in the 1700s. Introduced by William Jones in 1706, use of the symbol was popularized by Leonhard Euler, who adopted it in 1737.

An eighteenth-century French mathematician named Georges Buffon devised a way to calculate π based on probability. You can try it yourself at the Exploratorium's Pi Toss exhibit.
